==PAGE:4==
Abstract
This thesis presents the design and development of a modular, climate-controlled enclo-
sure system for High-Throughput Screening (HTS) in biological research applications.
The project was carried out in collaboration with DTU Arena for Life Science Automa-
tion (DALSA) to address the need for a flexible, reliable, and user-adaptable incubation
platform at the Technical University of Denmark (DTU). The system features a robust
insulated enclosure, precise temperature and humidity control, and a modular framework
that supports a variety of sample plate types and analytical modules. Challenges included
optimizing thermal performance, ensuring uniform climate conditions, and enabling rapid
module interchangeability through pogo pin and T-track connections. The electronics and
software, centered around a Raspberry Pi 5 and a Python based user interface, provide au-
tomated control, real-time monitoring, automated logging, and intuitive user interaction.
Experimental validation demonstrated that the system meets the majority of its Minimum
Viable Product (MVP) criteria, including a temperature range up to approximately 42°C,
uniformity within ±1°C and adjustable humidity up to 90%. The tests were conducted
with a few limitations regarding cooling eﬀiciency and humidifier placement, which may
require further optimization. The modular design allows for easy upgrades and integration
with laboratory automation, making the system a versatile platform for current and future
HTS workflows. This work establishes a foundation for further innovation in automated
life science instrumentation.

Modular Incubation and Monitoring System for Sample Storage in High-Throughput Screening
==PAGE:9==
1
Introduction
This report focuses on the development of a modular, incubation and monitoring sample
storage system for High-Throughput Screening (HTS) of biological samples.
HTS ad-
dresses the automation of imaging, incubation, and reproducibility, which allows large
scale testing of samples for biological activity.
The project, in collaboration with DTU Arena for Life Science Automation (DALSA)[1]
at the Technical University of Denmark (DTU), aims to develop a HTS system tailored
to the needs of researchers at DTU Bioengineering.
The overall system aims to automate three key features: imaging, storage, and incubation
of samples. The objective is to design a product capable of storing a large number of sample
plates, capturing high-quality images of each sample within a specified time interval, and
incubating the samples under controlled conditions. Although the process is intended to
be mostly automated, semi-automation will be the goal of the Minimum Viable Product
(MVP), requiring minimal human intervention.
This project builds on previous work that developed an automated storage system with a
robotic arm for transporting sample plates.[2] Some features of the machine are already
implemented and may require optimization, while others have not yet been considered.
Therefore, the project will entail both improving existing components and engineering new
solutions from scratch. The focus will primarily be on the following key areas:
• Enclosure: Designing an insulated enclosure that can accommodate all components
needed while providing a stable internal environment.
• Climate: Designing a climate-controlled system that ensures optimal temperature
and humidity for samples, improving incubation conditions.
• Modular Framework: Developing a robust, flexible, and modular system that can
accommodate different sample plate types, functions, and analytical modules.
The goal of this project is to achieve a functional MVP by expanding and improving the
existing system, with a strong emphasis on a modular design approach and the integration
of a climate-controlled enclosure to maintain optimal conditions for sample storage and
incubation.
1.1
Background
Humankind has been developing and analyzing biological samples for centuries.
The
invention of the first microscope in the late 16th century allowed scientists to observe
microorganisms and cells for the first time, marking the beginning of a new era in biology
and leading to significant advancements in our understanding of life sciences.[3]
Biological samples are fundamental to life science research, forming the basis for experi-
ments in microbiology, biotechnology, and medicine. The ability to cultivate and analyze a
wide variety of samples enables researchers to investigate cellular processes, genetic func-
tions, and the effects of different treatments. Among the most commonly studied and
cultivated organisms are mold fungi, yeast, Escherichia coli (E. coli), and Lactobacillus
species.
Mold fungi play a vital role in research and industry due to their involvement in decom-
position, antibiotic production, and enzyme synthesis. Yeasts, especially Saccharomyces
Modular Incubation and Monitoring System for Sample Storage in High-Throughput Screening
1
==PAGE:10==
cerevisiae, are indispensable in baking, brewing, and as model organisms for genetic and
cellular studies. E. coli is in molecular biology laboratories, valued for its rapid growth
and ease of genetic manipulation, making it the standard host for cloning and protein
expression. Lactobacillus species are crucial in food fermentation and probiotic research,
contributing to gut health and the development of functional foods.
Each of these organisms requires specific environmental conditions for optimal growth,
including temperature, humidity, and whether agitation (shaking) is necessary. Under-
standing and maintaining these conditions are essential for reproducibility and the success
of experimental workflows. Table 1.1 summarizes the optimal growth conditions for these
commonly used organisms.
Organism
Optimal Temp.

> 80%
No/Yes
Varies by experiment
Table 1.1: Optimal growth conditions for commonly cultivated laboratory organisms.[4]
The cultivation and monitoring of biological samples under controlled conditions are es-
sential for advancing the understanding of biological systems, enabling the development
of biotechnological applications, and ensuring reproducibility in experimental workflows.
Key environmental parameters play a critical role in this context:
• Temperature directly influences protein expression and growth rates.
• Humidity affects sample hydration, with low levels potentially altering metabolic
activity. (e.g., how cells generate energy, synthesize proteins, or grow)
• Shaking enhances oxygen availability in liquid cultures, impacting aerobic processes.
Consequently, quantitative replication of experiments requires a high degree of environ-
mental control.
1.2
High-Throughput Screening (HTS)
HTS is an automated experimental concept that enables the rapid analysis of large libraries
of chemical, biological, or biochemical compounds.[5] This significantly reduces the time
and labor required for the screening of compounds, making it a key technology in drug
discovery, material science, and biotechnology.[6]
HTS is widely used in pharmaceutical research to identify biologically active compounds,
known as ”hits,” which serve as the foundation for drug development. Hits can refer to a
new organism, specific optimal conditions, or the ideal DNA sequence, depending on the
research context. Since the 1990s, it has been fundamental in developing treatments for
cancer, infectious diseases, and neurodegenerative disorders.[6] Additionally, HTS plays
a growing role in functional genomics and biomarker discovery, enabling researchers to
analyze gene function and disease mechanisms more eﬀiciently.[7]
Advancements in automation, assay miniaturization, and computational analysis continue
to enhance HTS capabilities. The integration of artificial intelligence and machine learning
has further improved hit identification and predictive modeling, accelerating the pace of
scientific discovery.[8] As a result, high-throughput screening and analysis remains a crucial
tool for modern research, driving innovation in drug development.
2
Modular Incubation and Monitoring System for Sample Storage in High-Throughput Screening
==PAGE:11==
Reproducibility remains a critical issue in biological research. Baker (2016) reported that
more than 70% of the researchers failed to reproduce the findings of others and nearly
60% could not replicate their own results. This reproducibility problem arises from mul-
tiple factors, including variability in experimental procedures and human error, which
automation in biological laboratories can help mitigate by improving consistency and al-
lowing parallel experiments.[9] Automation in biological laboratories presents a promising
solution to mitigate this problem by reducing human error, ensuring consistent protocols,
and enhancing data reliability. Moreover, automation allows researchers to optimize time
management by conducting multiple experiments in parallel, improving throughput and
eﬀiciency.[10]
However, developing automated solutions for biolabs is a complex task due to the rapid
evolution of hardware and software technologies. Products can become obsolete quickly,
making it essential to design systems with adaptability in mind. A modular approach,
emphasizing reconfiguration and reuse of proven components, has been shown to reduce
the risk of premature obsolescence and extend product longevity.[11]
Another key challenge in laboratory automation is the handling processes before and after
an experiment. While automation has been introduced in various aspects of life science,
significant manual handling remains necessary. Two primary concerns arise from this.
Manual handling: To accommodate different biolab workflows, systems should allow
researchers to manually insert and remove samples. While full automation is the long-
term goal, ensuring compatibility with manual handling improve usability and adoption.
Non-standardized automation: The laboratory equipment industry is dominated by
multiple vendors, each using proprietary components and software, to maintain customer
dependence.[12] This lack of standardization complicates the integration of automated
systems from different manufacturers. The design for flexibility by supporting various
microplate formats significantly enhances the attractiveness and usability of a product.
1.3
DTU Arena for Life Science Automation (DALSA)
This project is conducted in collaboration with DALSA, a cross disciplinary initiative at
DTU that brings together students from electrical engineering, mechanical engineering,
and biotechnology to innovate within life science automation. DALSA serves as a collabo-
rative hub, supporting the development of new technologies and solutions for automation
in life science applications.
HTS is widely employed in biological research at DTU Bioengineering, such as in an
ongoing project screening a library of over 40,000 fungal species.[13] The current imaging
system, the Reshape Imaging System, has been identified as a limiting factor due to image
quality and reproducibility concerns. Through interviews with DTU Bioengineering staff,
key user requirements have been defined to guide the development of an improved imaging,
incubation, flexible, and storage system for HTS.
1.4
Market competition
The market for HTS systems with incubation capabilities is rapidly evolving, driven by
advances in automation, imaging technologies, and the increasing demand for eﬀicient
drug discovery processes. The global HTS market is projected to grow significantly, with
a Compound Annual Growth Rate (CAGR) of approximately 10.6% from 2023 to 2030,
reaching an estimated value of 41.67 billion by 2030.[14] This growth is fueled by the rising
need for HTS in drug discovery, personalized medicine, and genomics research.
Modular Incubation and Monitoring System for Sample Storage in High-Throughput Screening
3
==PAGE:12==
The HTS market is characterized by a diverse range of players, including established
pharmaceutical companies, biotechnology firms, and specialized technology providers. Key
trends driving the market include the increasing adoption of automation and robotics in
laboratories with advances in imaging technologies.
The demand for HTS systems is
driven by the need for faster and more eﬀicient drug discovery processes. The integration
of Artificial Intelligence (AI) and Machine Learning (ML) in HTS is also gaining traction,
enabling researchers to analyze large datasets and identify potential drug candidates more
effectively.
The market for HTS systems and incubators is highly competitive, with several established
players and emerging companies offering a range of solutions. Some of the key competitors
in the HTS market include:
• Thermo F. Scientific: A leading provider of laboratory equipment and reagents,
Thermo Fisher offers a variety of HTS systems, including the CellInsight CX5, which
combines imaging and analysis capabilities, and incubation systems such as the
Cytomat™2 Automated Incubator.
• Infors HT: The Multitron is a widely used incubator shaker system from Infors HT,
designed for high-throughput cultivation of microorganisms. It offers precise tem-
perature, humidity, and shaking control, making it suitable for a variety of biological
and biotechnological applications.
• Agilent Technologies: Agilent offers a range of HTS solutions, including the Bravo
Automated Liquid Handling Platform, which is designed for high-throughput sample
preparation and assay development.
• Tecan: Tecan specializes in laboratory automation and offers a variety of HTS sys-
tems, including the Fluent Automation Workstation, which provides flexible liquid
handling and integration with various detection technologies.
• Hamilton: Hamilton Robotics focuses on liquid handling automation and offers the
Microlab STAR system, which can be customized for various HTS applications.
• Reshape Imaging: Reshape Imaging is a company that specializes in HTS systems
for biological research. Their products are designed to enhance image quality and
reproducibility in HTS applications.
These competitors offer a range of HTS systems and incubators with varying features,
including imaging capabilities, heating capabilities, and automation. Different incubators
and imaging systems have been found and are shown in Figure 1.1.
The companies shown in Figure 1.1 are some of the key players in the HTS market,
each offering unique features and capabilities. They also have a wide range of products,
including incubators, imaging systems, and automation solutions, as Figure 1.1 only shows
a few examples of their incubators. Common to all of them is that they are not modular
by only offering a specific capability and lack implementation of the HTS system which is
growing in demand. If the use case slightly changes, e.g. needing more plates, automation,
new imaging system, integrated shaker, or other features, the user will need to buy a new
system for that new specific use case. This is a major drawback for users as they are
unable to adapt to their changing needs of the system under their research.
The Thermo F. Scientific Cytomat 2 incubator is the biggest competitor in automated
incubation. They collaborate with Tecan and Hamilton to provide large automated incu-
bation, allowing for a high number of samples to be incubated simultaneously. The Infors
4
Modular Incubation and Monitoring System for Sample Storage in High-Throughput Screening

HT multitron is a well established incubator shaker system, but it can only be used for
that specific purpose. The Agilent Technologies Bravo incubator can hold up to 8 plates,
but is limited to their automation system. The Tecan and Hamilton incubators are similar
to the Agilent Technologies incubator but are not as flexible and can only be used with
their own automation systems. The Reshape incubation and imaging system holds up to

other incubators are not publicly available, but are estimated to be in the same range as
the Agilent Technologies incubator at around the range of 20.000€ to 50.000€ one time
purchase.
The market for a modular, autonomous, incubation HTS system is clearly available, as
competitors are not able to offer a system that can be adapted to the needs of the user.
The system developed in this project will be able to offer a modular design with incubation,
allowing easy upgrades and changes to the system, while also being able to integrate with
other systems. This will make it a very attractive product for users, as they will be able
to adapt their system to their needs without having to buy a new expensive system.

The temperature range and humidity criteria are based on the most optimal growing

by assuming that the room temperature is below 25°C. However, if the room gets very
warm above 25°C on a hot summer day or a special experiment is being conducted, the

below room temperature, but this is not a requirement for the MVP as the focus is on the
most common use cases.

enclosure. Therefore, at least two temperature sensors should be used to ensure reliability

system and ensures that no contamination occurs between experiments. This reduces the
workload for users and ensures that the system is safe to use. The limitation of vibrations
is an additional requirement as it affects the results of the experiments.
In addition to the measurable criteria listed in Table 1.2, the machine should be considered
to be used in a cleanroom with Good Manufacturing Practices (GMP). It describes how
medical equipment and pharmaceutical products should be manufactured, tested, and
controlled, hence indirectly influencing the design of the machine. The machine should
be designed to be easy to clean, with smooth surfaces and minimal to no sharp edges,
to ensure that it can be cleaned properly.
The materials used should be resistant to

risk of contamination. Although this practice is used mainly for the production line, it
should be considered throughout the development of the machine. It is crucial to obtain
proper documentation on machine safety and will therefore be a long-term development
goal which is out of scope.[15]
1.6
Problem definition
Problem: How can an automated HTS system integrate incubation and a modular design
approach to improve versatility, enhance reproducibility, and growth conditions?
Purpose: The purpose of this project is to develop an enclosure with incubation and
interchangeable modules for an automated HTS system. By incorporating controlled in-
cubation conditions, the system aims to enhance experimental reproducibility and ensure
optimal sample environment conditions. The modular framework will improve versatil-
ity, allowing it to be adapted to various sample types and experimental workflows while
facilitating future upgrades.

2
Design Methodology
In the design methodology phase, ideas are formulated and transformed into structured
frameworks based on general engineering principles. Iterating and conceptualizing are a
crucial part of the development and a crucial factor to ensure a satisfactory prototype.
Market research, brainstorming with multiple biotech and mechanical students resulted
in a lot of desired features and ideas for the HTS machine. This resulted in the idea of a
modular framework, where the enclosure with incubation is the base of the system, and
the modules are interchangeable subassemblies. All modules should be data-driven and
have the same communication protocol, allowing for easy integration and expansion of the
system. The modules should be easy to change for the user without experience and should
be able to run with different modules at the same time.
2.1
Verification triangle in mechanical design
Through work and experience, Verification Triangle has proven to be a valuable framework
for connecting all mechanical design aspects. Computer Aided Design (CAD) is the use of
software to create designs based on theories, which together result in a physical prototype
where the initial theory can be tested and validated. The triangle consists of these three
primary vertices: CAD, Theory, and Prototype. Each edge represents a critical link that
ensures consistency and validation between stages.
CAD
Theory
Prototype
Simulation
Measurement
Test
• CAD – Theory: Connected through
Simulation, where theoretical princi-
ples are applied and tested within the
virtual model.
• CAD – Prototype: Linked via Mea-
surement, ensuring that the physical
build aligns with the digital design.
• Theory – Prototype:
Bridged by
Testing, validating theoretical predic-
tions through experimental results.
Figure 2.1: Verification triangle in mechanical design
This structured approach facilitates iterative development, promotes design validation at
each stage, and ensures that the final product meets both functional and theoretical expec-
tations. Throughout this report, the Verification Triangle will serve as a guiding framework
for designing and analyzing mechanical designs. The sections will cover conceptual de-
velopment, detailed modeling, simulation, prototyping, and experimental validation. This
not only clarifies the rationale behind design decisions, but also demonstrates a systematic
approach to ensuring reliability and performance. In summary, the Verification Triangle
will be used to justify methodologies and interpret results throughout the thesis.

2.2
Structure design
The initial concept development of the basic structure and space allocations was made
in an earlier project[2]. This machine follows the same main design structure, but adds
a modular framework, and climate control with an enclosed structure ensuring stable
temperature and humidity. An overview of the machine concept is shown in Figure 2.2.
Frame
Modular framework
Electronic housing
Air recircula on
Robot transporta on
Front sec on
Le secon
Figure 2.2: Machine concept overview
The machine consists of a main frame with panels, which is the main structure of the ma-
chine. Within the walls of the frame are insulation, which is used to keep the temperature
and humidity stable and to reduce the energy consumption.
Inside of the enclosure is the modular framework, which is used to hold the modules
in place.
They can be can accommodate different functions such as storage, shaking,
imaging and more. Modules are interchangeable parts of the machine that can be swapped
depending on the application and the needs of the user. The modules should be easy to
change out for the user with no experience, and the machine should be able to run with
different modules at the same time with a total of 10 modules mounted. The modules are
mounted in two rows of five modules, which simplifies the design and allows easy access
for the end user.
The modules are stacked on top of the electronic housing at the bottom of the machine
in Figure 2.2. Communication from modules, sensors, fans, etc., is routed down to the
electronic housing, where the main control unit is located. From the electronic housing, the
climate heaters, coolers, humidifiers, and fans are placed at the bottom of the machine,
where it circulates air through and into the enclosure. The air is then distributed up
through the modules and back down to the electronic housing within the side walls.
The robotic transportation system is mounted at the front of the machine and is used to
transport the samples between modules. The robotic system has already been developed
in a previous project and is not part of this project.[2] But it should be redesigned to fit
the new machine as this design structure requires the robotic system to be symmetrical
to reach both sides of the machine. The development of this new robotic system is being
done in parallel with this project but is out of scope for this project.
2.3
Parametric modeling principle
Parametric modeling is the use of CAD software to create, modify, analyze, and optimize
designs. Onshape was chosen as the CAD software as it allows you to easily share and work
on the same parts and assemblies, while keeping everything in the cloud. This allows any
10
Modular Incubation and Monitoring System for Sample Storage in High-Throughput Screening
==PAGE:19==
computer and team member to work and collaborate across products. This is crucial for
the development of this machine, as multiple aspects of the machine are being developed
in parallel with this project.
2.3.1
Skeleton modeling
Skeleton modeling is a technique used in parametric CAD design to create a structural
framework that defines the overall shape and relationships of a model before adding de-
tailed features. It serves as a foundation for complex designs with multiple parts, allowing
for better control, flexibility, and consistency throughout the design process. The master
skeleton for the machine is shown in Figure 2.3.
Figure 2.3: Master skeleton
In skeleton modeling, a master sketch or reference geometry, such as planes, axes, and
key dimensions, is used to establish the fundamental structure. This framework acts as
a guide, ensuring that all dependent components follow the intended design intent. By
using parametric relationships, engineers can link different parts to the skeleton so that
modifications to the main framework automatically update all associated elements. This
approach is used as a top-down design, so the main skeleton controls the most important
dimensions of the machine.
One of the biggest advantages of skeleton modeling is its ability to simplify design changes.
Instead of modifying multiple components individually, adjustments can be made to the
skeleton, propagating updates throughout the model. This not only improves eﬀiciency,
but also reduces the risk of errors. Additionally, skeleton modeling enhances collaboration
by allowing different team members to work on separate parts while maintaining alignment
with the overall structure.
By providing a structured approach to complex modeling, skeleton modeling ensures better
Modular Incubation and Monitoring System for Sample Storage in High-Throughput Screening
11
==PAGE:20==
control over design intent, making it a powerful tool when working on large or intricate
projects.
2.4
Robust design principles
Robust design approaches have been utilized throughout the design phase. The approach
focuses on creating products and systems that perform consistently under varying condi-
tions. The goal is to minimize sensitivity to variations caused by manufacturing tolerances,
environmental factors, and usage differences, ensuring reliability and quality.
A robust design process involves identifying key parameters that influence performance
and making the system less susceptible to external disturbances. This is achieved through
dimensional tolerances and kinematic constrains evaluations. By understanding how vari-
ations affect the final product, it is possible to make design choices that reduce defects
and improve overall reliability.
This method helps reduce the impact of uncontrollable noise factors, such as temperature
changes, material inconsistencies, or user behavior. Instead of trying to eliminate these
variations entirely, which often leads to expensive manufacturing, a robust design ensures
that their effect on performance is minimized.
Robust design principles have many aspects, including kinematic constraints, dimensional
tolerances, Design for Manufacturing (DFM) and Design for Assembly (DFA).
These
principles are applied to the design of the machine to ensure that it is robust and reliable.
An example of this is the kinematic constraints used to ensure that the parts fit together
correctly and reduce the risk of misalignment during assembly. This is done by using
different hole geometries, which is just as easy to make as regular holes on a plasma
cutter. The hole concept is demonstrated in Figure 2.4.
Standard Design
CAD World
Everything is perfect
Robust Design
Production
Variation exist
Rz
x
y
Figure 2.4: Kinematic constraints of CAD vs Production
The figure shows a standard and robust design example of two connected cylinders (brown)
that should be fitted into a plate (blue). It is demonstrated in the ”CAD World” where
everything fits perfectly, and in the ”Production” where dimensional tolerances exist.
Figure 2.4 shows how the robust design approach fulfills the kinematic constraints, while
the standard design does not. The robust design uses the kinematic constraints of having
the left hole control the vertical and horizontal positions (x and y) of the cylinder, whereas
the other hole to the right is long and therefore only controls the rotation (Rz) of the
cylinder assembly.
This allows the robust design to be more forgiving to dimensional
tolerances compared to the standard design, which has a tight fit in all three directions
on both cylinders.
12
Modular Incubation and Monitoring System for Sample Storage in High-Throughput Screening
==PAGE:21==
This approach follows the kinematic analysis procedure Kutzbach/Grübler crition, which
is used to determine the degrees of freedom in a mechanical system. The formula for 2D
planar mechanisms is given by: B = 3(n −1) −∑Ui. Where B is mobility (degrees of
freedom), n is the number of links (including the frame) and Ui is the number of constraints
imposed by each joint or connection.
The formula can be used to determine the number of degrees of freedom in a mechanical
system, where B should be 0 for a fully constrained system, which is the case for this
stationary machine. Some panels or components might have negative B indicating over-
constraints, as some are large and flexible, which is not reflected in the CAD model. The
approach is not explicitly stated later in the report, but is used throughout the design
to minimize the number of overconstraints, allowing for a more robust design that can
handle variations in manufacturing and assembly.
2.4.1
DFM and DFA
In order to ensure that the design can be manufactured and is easy to assemble, Design
for Manufacturing (DFM) and Design for Assembly (DFA) principles have been applied
throughout the design process.
DFM focuses on simplifying the manufacturing process by considering factors such as
material selection, tolerances, and production methods. This approach aims to reduce
production costs, minimize waste, and improve product quality by designing components
that are easy to manufacture. This is why the machine is designed with sheet metal plates
and aluminum extrusions. Sheet metal is easy to manufacture and can be cut and bent
to the desired shape, allowing large custom panels to be made using a plasma cutter.
Aluminum extrusions are also easy to manufacture and can be cut to the desired length
with a unique profile that allows easy assembly and mounting of the panels. This reduces
the need for complex machining processes and allows for quick and eﬀicient production.
DFA emphasizes designing products that are easy to assemble, reducing the number of
parts, and minimizing assembly time. This is achieved by designing components that can
be easily aligned, inserted, and fastened together, which is why robust design principles
have been applied to the design of the machine. The use of kinematic constraints and
certain tight tolerances ensures that the parts fit together correctly and reduces the risk
of misalignment during assembly. This approach not only speeds up the assembly process
but also improves product reliability by minimizing the chances of assembly errors.
Modular Incubation and Monitoring System for Sample Storage in High-Throughput Screening
13
==PAGE:22==
14
Modular Incubation and Monitoring System for Sample Storage in High-Throughput Screening
==PAGE:23==
3
Enclosure
The purpose of the enclosure is to isolate the internal climate from the external envi-
ronment, providing a stable climate for the samples. It should be designed to maintain a
constant temperature and humidity level, which is critical for the growth and development
of biological samples. The enclosure must also be designed to prevent contamination from
outside sources, such as dust, bacteria, and other pollutants.
All this while still being easy and versatile to mount internal components, such as heaters,
fans, and sensors, so further development can be done in the future. It must be designed
to allow for easy access to these components for maintenance and repair.
The enclosure is developed with the CAD program Onshape, using the master skeleton
model shown in Figure 2.3. The final enclosure is shown in Figure 3.1 with a front view
and a back view with the electronic plate pulled out.
(a) Front view
(b) Back view with demounted electronic plate
Figure 3.1: Final CAD model of the enclosure
3.1
Frame
The main frame of the enclosure is made of 40x40 aluminum extrusions, which are lightweight
and easy to work with, allowing easy assembly and disassembly. This removes the need for
welding, which can be time consuming and expensive, while also allowing for easy mod-
ifications in the future. All extrusions are bought from myaluprofil, which is a german
supplier of aluminum extrusions and accessories.
From the Skeleton Model shown in Figure 2.3, the extrusions are configured to the desired
lengths following the dynamic dimensioning of the skeleton.
The frame is assembled
in its final form using corner brackets and screws as seen in Figure 3.2a, allowing easy
adjustments and modifications in the future. Mate connectors are also shown in Figure
3.2a, which are used to connect door hinges to the frame, allowing for a revolute joint in
CAD, replicating the dynamic behavior of the door.
Modular Incubation and Monitoring System for Sample Storage in High-Throughput Screening
15
==PAGE:24==
The frame seen in Figure 3.2a consists of 9 rounded extrusions with threaded holes in
each end, which are held together with corner brackets and screws. At the bottom of the
frame, there are 6 extrusions with a closed groove mounted together with brackets and
screws ensuring that the frame is stable.
(a) Frame assembly
(b) Door assemblies
Figure 3.2: Frame and door assemblies of the enclosure
3.2
Doors
Following the same principle as the frame, the doors are seen in Figure 3.2b are made
from slightly smaller 30x30 aluminum extrusions, to make room for the windows, which
are made from 3mm UV resistant polycarbonate sheets.
From Figure 3.2b it is seen that the front utilizes different extrusion profiles. The top and
bottom extrusions are 30x30 profiles with a closed groove to the front and edge of the
door, also extending the plane face out to the ends of the side extrusions with its caps.
This is done to make the door look clean from the front and to allow a silicone seal to
lie flush with the edge of the door frame on the top and bottom. The extrusions on each
side of the doors are also made of 30x30 profiles with one closed groove, with one of the
extrusions having an extra closed groove for the silicone rubber seal to lay against when
the door is closed.
The door is equipped with handles and magnets to ensure it stays flush with the frame
when closed. It is designed with removable hinges that allow easy access to the internal
components of the enclosure.
Polycarbonate (PC) sheets are mounted on each side of the door with countersunk M4
screws, providing an air gap with good insulation while not degrading from potential UV
light but blocking against UV light. PC is also a strong and durable material that is
resistant to impact and shattering, making it an ideal choice for windows.
The front windows are mounted with small 3D printed brackets in each corner to make
the front window flush with the door frame, while the inner windows are slightly larger
16
Modular Incubation and Monitoring System for Sample Storage in High-Throughput Screening
==PAGE:25==
to make it easy to mount to the door extrusions. Both windows are sealed with silicone
rubber to prevent any air leaks, ensuring a tight seal.
3.3
Plates
To fully enclose the frame, plates are mounted on each side of the frame, with the exception
of the front, where the doors are mounted. The enclosure consists of two layers of plates,
an outer and an inner layer, with insulation in between.
The plates have a thickness
of 2mm or 3mm, depending on the load applied to them. Those who are loaded with
modules use 3mm plates, while the rest use 2mm plates. The plates are designed to be
easy to assemble and disassemble, allowing easy access to the internal components of the
enclosure.
All plates are cut on a plasma cutter and bend afterwards with the help of a CNC bending
machine.
The cutter and bending machine on DTU is not able to handle more than
1200mm in both directions, which was a bit too small for most of the plates, with the
largest dimension being 1426mm on one plate. In order to solve this, the machining was
outsourced and multiple offers were gathered from different suppliers. This was done to
ensure the best price and quality for the plates, as well as to ensure that the plates were
cut and bent to the correct dimensions without any compromises in the design.
3.3.1
Outer plates
The outer plates are mainly constructed from a 2mm plate with flat surfaces and flanges
on two ends with screw holes for mounting to the frame as seen in Figure 3.3.
(a) Outer plate front view
(b) Outer plate back view
Figure 3.3: Outer plates mounted on the frame
The left and right plates have a cutout for the present plate function, where an external
robot should be able to insert a plate with a sample into the enclosure. The bottom plate
also has three holes for the climate units to exchange heat with the outside environment
if cooling is needed.
3.3.2
Inner plates
The inner plates are intended to be the first layer of insulation by holding the insulation in
place. It is also used to mount the internal components, such as the heater, fans, module
T-tracks and sensors. All plates are shown in Figure 3.4 mounted on the frame.

The inner left plate has cutouts for the tangential fan on the top left of Figure 3.5a that

The base and module plate has mounting points for the climate units, consisting of the

through from the screen, sensors, and pogo pin connectors.
The module plate has screw holes for the T-tracks, which are used to slide in the modules
in the enclosure. It is mounted to the back of the frame and the base plate with one
positioning hole in each, with the rest being fastening holes, which is larger than the
screws to allow for some tolerance in the assembly.
The base plate has a cutout for the climate module where the cooling unit sticks out to
expel heat. It is mounted to the frame around the edge to ensure a stable base for the
enclosure. At the front of the base plate, there is an interlock cutout for a front plate so
the isolation stays in place.
3.3.3
Robust design applied
All the panels on the machine need to be mounted together to make a fully enclosed
incubator with sandwich construction. This is done mainly using M6 screws with a tight
tolerance in a few holes, while others have a larger hole. The large Ø8 holes clamp the
plates to the mounting surface of the extrusions, while the closely fitted Ø6.2 holes clamp
and align the plate to the rest of the structure, as seen in Figure 3.7.
Modular Incubation and Monitoring System for Sample Storage in High-Throughput Screening
19
==PAGE:28==
Figure 3.7: Kinematic constraints on inner top plate to extrusions in 2D
There are three tight holes as the screws are mounted to the aluminum grooves, which
only allows for precise fitting in two directions, the surface itself and the perpendicular
direction of the groove. Note that Rx and Ry are not stated as it is not a concern in this
case.
Instead, when two plates have to be fitted, only two holes are kinematically optimal for
the kinematic constrain definition. One tight fitted hole defines the two directions in the
2D plane, while the long hole defines the rotation. This is assuming that parts are rigid
bodies and do not flex, which is often not reflected in the real world. Due to flex in many
materials, multiple z constraints have been used, e.g. in Figure 3.7 and all other parts.
Another example is the module base mounted on the base plate, which is done using a
hole and a long hole approach, as seen in Figure 3.8.
Figure 3.8: Kinematic constraints on module base mounted to the base plate in 2D
The module base mounted to the base plate is indexed in the left hole in Figure 3.8. It
is over-constrained as there are two long holes in the middle and to the right, where only
one should be needed to lock in the rotation. But as mentioned earlier, the material might
flex leading to misalignment along the plates contact surface, which is compensated for
with this one additional y alignment feature.
These robust design principles are used on all 22 sheet metal plates to reduce the number
of defects, lower costs, and enhance the end product satisfaction. This approach leads
to products that maintain high performance even in unpredictable real-world conditions,
ultimately improving durability and longevity.

3.4
Material selection
When selecting materials for the enclosure, both aluminum and stainless steel were con-
sidered due to their suitability for incubators and clean-room environments. The most
common materials used for enclosures in these applications are 6061 aluminum, 304 and
316 stainless steel. The properties of these materials are summarized in Table 3.1.

6061 Aluminum is lightweight and easy to machine, making it a viable option if properly
coated or anodized to prevent corrosion. However, its susceptibility to cleaning agents and
humid environments limits its use in sterile or clean-room applications.
304 stainless steel offers good corrosion resistance and is more affordable than 316
stainless steel, making it suitable for general purpose applications. However, it is less
resistant to chlorides and harsh cleaning agents.
316 stainless steel, with its molybdenum content, provides superior corrosion resistance,
especially in humid or chemically aggressive environments. It is the ideal choice for clean
rooms and incubation enclosures where long-term sterility and durability are critical.
In summary, while aluminum can be used in less demanding environments, stainless steel
is the preferred choice for incubators and clean rooms because of its superior corrosion
resistance, durability, and ease of sterilization. The choice between 304 and 316 stainless
steel depends on the specific application. For incubators and clean rooms, 316 stainless
steel is mostly used due to its excellent resistance to harsh cleaning agents and humidity.
As the goal of this thesis is to make an MVP with a limited budget, 304 stainless steel is a
cost-effective alternative, as its exposure to strong chemicals is minimal for this prototype.
For these reasons, 304 stainless steel was chosen for the enclosure.
3.4.1
Heat expansion considerations
When combining aluminum extrusions for the frame and stainless steel plates for the
enclosure, it is important to consider the difference in thermal expansion coeﬀicients. The
linear expansion coeﬀicient for 6061 aluminum is αAlu = 23 · 10−6 K−1, while for 304
stainless steel it is αSteel = 17.3 · 10−6 K−1. [16]
For the longest dimension of the enclosure, W = 1354 mm and a maximum expected
temperature difference of ∆T = 35 K, the thermal expansion can be calculated as:
∆LAlu = αAlu · W · ∆T = 23 · 10−6 K−1 · 1354 mm · 35 K = 1.09 mm
(3.1)
∆LSteel = αSteel · W · ∆T = 17.3 · 10−6 K−1 · 1354 mm · 35 K = 0.82 mm
(3.2)
This results in a worst-case expansion difference of approximately 0.27 mm over the full
width of the enclosure. This small difference is within the tolerances of the mounting holes
Modular Incubation and Monitoring System for Sample Storage in High-Throughput Screening
21
==PAGE:30==
and the flexibility of the assembly, since there are small gaps with foam insulation between
the plates and extrusions. The plates and frame are therefore not rigidly constrained in a
way that would cause significant stress or deformation as a result of this expansion.
To evaluate the risk of thermal fatigue due to differential thermal expansion between
aluminum (6061) and stainless steel (304), stresses have been calculated and evaluated
using Hookes law, which states that stress in a material is proportional to strain. The
thermal strain resulting from the mismatch in coeﬀicients of thermal expansion is given
by:
∆ε = (αAlu −αSteel) · ∆T = (23 · 10−6 −17.3 · 10−6) K−1 · 35 K = 199.5 · 10−6
(3.3)
Assuming full mechanical restraint, thermal stress is given by Hooke’s Law:
σAlu = EAlu · ∆ε = 69 GPa · 199.5 · 10−6 ≈13.8 MPa
(3.4)
σSteel = ESteel · ∆ε = 193 GPa · 199.5 · 10−6 ≈38.5 MPa
(3.5)
The typical fatigue properties for these materials are:
• 6061 Aluminum: σf ≈96.5 MPa at N = 5 · 108 [17]
• 304 Stainless Steel: σf ≈240 MPa at N = 5 · 108 [18]
Since both σAlu and σSteel are significantly below the fatigue strength, the risk of fatigue is
low and they can withstand more than N = 5 · 108 cycles without failure according to the
S-N curve for these materials. Under normal operating loads and temperature changes,
this expansion difference will not affect the structural function of the enclosure.
3.5
Insulation
The enclosure must also be designed to be energy eﬀicient, as it will be operating contin-
uously for long periods of time. This means that the materials used in the construction
of the enclosure must have good insulation properties to minimize heat loss and energy
consumption.
Doors should be insulated to prevent heat loss, but as they should be transparent, enclosed
air is used as insulation. In general, insulation is used instead of just air because insulating
materials have lower thermal conductivity and reduce heat transfer more effectively. Air
is a good insulator, but only when it is stationary.
If air circulates freely, convection
will significantly increase heat transfer. Insulating materials, such as foam, glass wool, or
mineral wool, perform better because they:
• Contain small air pockets, which limit convection.
• Have low thermal conductivity, reducing heat loss.
To allow clear visibility into the enclosure, enabling observation of both the modules
and the movement of the robotic arm(out of scope), the doors must be transparent. To
meet this requirement, air is used as the insulating layer between the two polycarbonate
windows, providing a reduction in heat transfer. The enclosure is insulated with material
between the inner and outer plates. Different insulation materials have been considered
and their properties compared based on thermal conductivity.
3.5.1

heat transfer through the enclosure walls was calculated using standard thermal resis-
tance formulas. The enclosure’s internal dimensions and surface area were used for these

BEWI G80 EPS is a type of expanded polystyrene (EPS) insulation that is lightweight,
inexpensive, and has good thermal insulation properties. It is commonly used in construc-
tion and packaging applications. However, it is flammable and has limited resistance to
moisture. ROCKWOOL Prolit is a type of stone wool insulation that is non-combustible,
has excellent thermal and acoustic insulation properties, and is resistant to moisture and
mold. It is commonly used in construction and industrial applications. However, it is
more expensive than EPS and may not be as easy to work with. ArmaFlex is a type of
flexible elastomeric foam insulation that is designed for use in HVAC and refrigeration ap-
plications. It has excellent thermal insulation properties, is resistant to moisture and mold
with its Microban technology, which inhibits the growth of bacteria and mold. However,
it is more expensive than both EPS and ROCKWOOL.
Rockwool was chosen for the insulation of the enclosure, as it has an excellent thermal to
price ratio, is non-combustible, and has good resistance to moisture and mold. To ensure
that no bacteria or mold can grow in the insulation, the insulation is layered with aluminum
foil on one side and adhered to the inner plates with double sided tape. Furthermore, the
insulation is taped around the edges to the inner plates with aluminum tape to prevent
any moisture from entering the insulation.

4
Climate
The climate system is a critical component of the enclosure, as it is responsible for main-
taining the desired temperature and humidity levels. The system consists of several com-
ponents, including heating and cooling elements, a humidifier, and a fan. The design of
the climate system is based on the requirements for the enclosure shown in Table 1.2,
which include maintaining and adjusting the temperature and humidity.
4.1
Heating and cooling element
There mainly exist two widely used directions when choosing an element which can com-
pose both heating and cooling, namely a Peltier module, a Vapor-Compression Refrigera-
tion (VCR) or a combination of these and other elements.
A VCR system is a common choice for heating and cooling applications, as it is very
eﬀicient and can provide a large temperature difference. It is also very easy to control,
as it can be turned on and off with a simple switch or relay. The downside is that it is
bulky, advanced and requires moving parts (compressor) with refrigerant management.
The Peltier module is a solid-state device that transfers heat using the Peltier effect.
It is compact and can be used for both heating and cooling, but is generally not as
eﬀicient as a VCR system. A combination of a Peltier element and a Positive Temperature
Coeﬀicient (PTC) heater can be used to provide both heating and cooling, while being
compact and easy to control. This combination allows for a more eﬀicient and compact
design, as the Peltier element can be used to cool the air while the PTC heater can be
used to heat the air.
In addition to these two, a PTC heater is also considered because it is a good option for
heating the enclosure, as it is self-regulating. A resistive heater is also considered for its
simplicity and ease of use for heating. To determine the right heating and cooling element,
a pugh chart was used to decide on the best option, in Table 4.1.
Peltier

Table 4.1: Pugh chart for the different heating and cooling elements. The scoring is based
on a scale from 1-5, where 1 is the worst and 5 is the best.
A description of the different criteria and ranking is given in the following list, based on
general use cases in the industry.
Modular Incubation and Monitoring System for Sample Storage in High-Throughput Screening
27
==PAGE:36==
• Heating eﬀiciency: The VCR is the best option for heating, with a high eﬀiciency
of up to 6. The PTC and resistive heater is also a good option, with an eﬀiciency of
around 1. The Peltier module is not as eﬀicient, with an eﬀiciency of around 0.5.
• Cooling eﬀiciency: The VCR is the best option for cooling, with an eﬀiciency of
up to 6. The Peltier module is not as eﬀicient, with an eﬀiciency of around 0.5. The
PTC and resistive heater is not used for cooling.
• Heating/Cooling capacity: The VCR has the largest capacity, It is used in homes
and in the industry for large applications. The PTC and resistive heater is also a
good option, but has a smaller capacity. The Peltier module is not as eﬀicient and
is used for small applications.
• Temperature difference: The VCR can provide the largest temperature differ-
ence, as it can be used for both heating and cooling. The PTC and resistive heater
can also provide large temperature differences, but only heating. The Peltier module
can provide a small temperature difference, as the temperature difference is limited
by the Peltier effect.
• Size: The Peltier module is the smallest option, as it is a solid-state device. The
PTC and resistive heater are also compact, but not as small as the Peltier module.
The VCR is the largest option and bulkiest option, as it requires a compressor,
condenser, expansioner and evaporator.
• Cost: The Peltier module is the cheapest option, as it is a solid-state device. The
PTC and resistive heater are also cheap, as they are widely used.
The VCR is
the most expensive option, as it requires a compressor, condenser, expansioner and
evaporator, which is packed into one package.
• Ease of use: The Peltier and PTC element is the easiest option to use, as it can
be turned on and off using a switch/relay. The resistive heater is also easy to use
but requires extra monitoring of the temperature and electric current. The VCR
is the most diﬀicult option to use, as it has multiple components that need to be
monitored and controlled.
• Maintenance: All options are relatively easy to maintain, but the VCR might be
the most diﬀicult option to maintain as it has the most components that can fail
and refrigerant that can leak.
• Safety: The Peltier module and PTC heater is the safest options, as they do not
have moving parts and do not require any refrigerant. The resistive heater is also
safe, but requires monitoring of the temperature and electric current. The VCR
is the least safe option, as it has multiple components that can fail and requires
refrigerant management that can leak.
Based on the score in Table 4.1, the VCR is the best option for both heating and cooling,
but is very complex and bulky. The Peltier module is not as eﬀicient as the VCR, but it
is compact and easy to use. The PTC and resistive heater is a good option for heating,
with the PTC being the safest to use.
From an evaluation based on Table 4.1, the criteria of Table 1.2 and the small space
constraint of the enclosure design, the Peltier module is chosen as the best option for
cooling, while the PTC heater is chosen as the best option for heating. The PTC heater is
inherently self-regulating and can operate without additional control systems when only
its maximum heating temperature is required. However, since the initial requirements
28
Modular Incubation and Monitoring System for Sample Storage in High-Throughput Screening

specify the need for adjustable temperatures, individual controllers are implemented to
enable precise temperature regulation of both units.
The specific ordered PTC unit and Peltier module is to be seen on Figure 4.1a and 4.1b,
respectively. They were specifically chosen because they are cheap, compact and available
for fast shipping. The Peltier module comes with a heatsink, and both of them come
with fans, which is a big advantage as it helps distribute the heat and cold air evenly
throughout the enclosure.
(a) PTC heater
(b) Peltier module
Figure 4.1: PTC heater and Peltier module used in the enclosure.
4.1.1
Peltier module
A Peltier module, also known as a Thermoelectric Cooler (TEC), is a solid-state device
that transfers heat using the Peltier effect. Peltier modules are widely used in applications
that require precise temperature control, such as electronics cooling, biomedical devices,
and laboratory equipment. A Peltier module consists of an array of P- and N-type semi-
conductor junctions arranged between two ceramic plates. The P-type semiconductors
have extra spaces for electrons, known as holes, which carry a positive charge, while the
N-type semiconductors have extra electrons, which carry a negative charge. When a Direct
Current (DC) voltage is applied, electrons in the N-type material move to the holes in the
P-type material across the junctions. Meaning, they absorb heat on one side and release it
on the other, creating a temperature gradient, resulting in one side of the module cooling
down while the opposite side heats up.
Peltier modules are favored for their compact size, silent operation, and precise thermal
regulation.
Common applications include cooling of CPU and electronic components,
portable refrigeration, medical instruments such as PCR machines, and laser diode tem-
perature stabilization. However, their eﬀiciency is limited by internal resistive heating
and heat leakage, which require effective heat dissipation methods, such as heat sinks or
active cooling systems, to optimize performance.[21]
The Coeﬀicient of Performance (COP) is a key performance metric for Peltier modules,
defined as the ratio of heat removed from the cold side to the electrical power consumed.
The COP is highest when the temperature difference is small and the current is low. As
the current increases, the COP decreases as more power is consumed to maintain the
temperature difference. This means that the Peltier module is most eﬀicient when used
Modular Incubation and Monitoring System for Sample Storage in High-Throughput Screening
29
==PAGE:38==
for small temperature differences and low currents. The COP can be improved by using
multiple Peltier modules in parallel or in series, but this also increases the complexity and
cost of the system.
As the current increases, the Peltier modules stabilize around a COP of 0.5, indicating
that the use of multiple modules may be more effective than increasing the current to
improve performance.[22] Although Peltier modules are not highly eﬀicient, their compact
size and ease of implementation make them a practical choice for this tightly integrated
enclosure application.

used are summarized in Table 4.2.

In order to estimate the number of Peltier modules needed, some assumptions are used:
• The inner wall of the enclosure is assumed to be equal to the air temperature of the
enclosure, since steady-state applies (Time →∞).
• The outer wall of the enclosure uses convection to the ambient temperature.
• The heat leakage from the enclosure to the ambient environment is modeled as a
linear function of the temperature difference (∆T) between the enclosure and the
ambient environment.
• The cooling eﬀiciency decreases as the temperature difference (∆T) increases.
The power P consumed by each Peltier module is calculated as:
P = V · I = 12 V · 6 A = 72 W
(4.1)
where V is the voltage and I is the current. The thermal mass of the air in the enclosure

4.1.2
PTC heater
A Positive Temperature Coeﬀicient (PTC) heater is a type of electric heating element
that utilizes the PTC effect to provide self-regulating heating. Unlike traditional resistive
heaters, which can overheat and require external temperature control, PTC heaters auto-
matically adjust their resistance based on temperature changes. This characteristic makes
them safe and eﬀicient for various applications, including space heating, automotive, and
industrial processes.
The PTC effect occurs in certain materials, such as barium titanate (BaTiO3), which
exhibit a significant increase in resistance as the temperature increases. When a voltage
is applied to a PTC heater, the current flows through the material, generating heat. As
the temperature increases, the resistance also increases, reducing the current flow and
limiting further heating. This self-regulating behavior prevents overheating and ensures
stable operation.
The PTC heater’s self-regulating behavior allows it to maintain a stable temperature
without the need for complex control and safety systems. PTC heaters are widely used in
applications such as space heating, automotive defrosting, and industrial processes. They
offer several advantages, including:
• Safety and Reliability: The self-regulating feature prevents overheating, reducing
the risk of fire hazards.
• Energy Eﬀiciency: PTC heaters consume less energy at maximum power com-
pared to traditional resistive heaters, as they adjust their current draw based on its
temperature.
• Compact Design: PTC heaters can be made in various shapes and sizes, making
them suitable for space-constrained applications.
• Durability: PTC materials are resistant to thermal cycling and mechanical stress,
ensuring a long service life.
However, PTC heaters also have some limitations, such as a slower response time in
certain cases compared to traditional heaters and a limited temperature range. Despite
these drawbacks, their self-regulating nature and safety features make them an attractive
option for many heating applications.[21]
The PTC heater is a good option for the enclosure as it is self-regulating and does not
require additional control systems. It is also very compact and can be placed in many
different locations. The only downside is that it is not as eﬀicient as a VCR system, but
32
Modular Incubation and Monitoring System for Sample Storage in High-Throughput Screening
==PAGE:41==
it is much easier to implement and does not require any moving parts. The PTC heater
is also very easy to control, as it can be turned on and off with a simple switch or relay.
In order to estimate the number of PTC heaters needed, the same procedure and assump-
tions as for the Peltier module are used. The parameters used are summarized in Table

4.1.3
PTC heater testing
To estimate the temperature of the PTC units an Optris PI 640i Infrared Camera with
Optris PIX Connect has been used. It is an infrared camera with a resolution of 640×480
pixels and a temperature range of −20 to 900°C.
The camera is used to measure the temperature of the PTC units during operation, and
the data is recorded using the Optris PIX Connect software. The camera is calibrated to
ensure accurate temperature measurements. The camera is placed at a distance of 30cm
from the PTC units and set to record at a frame rate of 30 frames per second, with the
data recorded for a period of one cycle of heating and the subsequent cool down.
The PTC units are powered by a 24V power supply, and the current is measured using a
multimeter. The power supply is set to provide a constant voltage of 24V, and the current
is measured during operation to ensure that the PTC units are operating within their
specified limits. An inferred image is shown in Figure 4.4a, where the PTC unit reached
its max temperature of around 250°C.
