From the image, it can be seen that the PTC unit is not uniform in temperature, while
the maximum temperature converges to 250°C. This temperature is relatively high, but
the blown air from the fan is not as hot. This shows that the upper temperature criterion
of 42°C is well within the limits of the PTC unit. The air temperature is lower than the
PTC unit seen in Figure 4.4a and will be adjusted by a feedback loop to ensure that the
air temperature is at the desired level.
In order to ensure that the desired temperature is reached fast and sustain it stable, the
PTC unit is controlled by the Raspberry Pi, which measures the temperature of the air
in the enclosure and adjusts the output accordingly. It has two ways of adjusting the
temperature of the enclosure, by rapidly turning on and off to emulate a Pulse Width
Modulation (PWM) signal of the PTC unit or the attached fan. PWM is a technique
used to control the power delivered to electrical devices by rapidly switching the power on
34
Modular Incubation and Monitoring System for Sample Storage in High-Throughput Screening
==PAGE:43==
and off. This allows for precise control of the average power delivered to the device. By
utilizing this technique the PTC unit can be controlled to maintain a stable temperature
in the enclosure, while also ensuring that air is circulated evenly throughout the enclosure.
The PTC surface heater profile is monitored up to its maximum temperature of 250°C
and shown in Figure 4.4b. The PTC unit is turned on from the ambient air temperature
of 26°C to the maximum temperature of 250°C, which is reached almost linearly, as the
power to heat ratio is 1:1, until the material resistance increases at maximum temperature.
It was sitting at maximum temperature for 1 minute before power was cut at around 2-3
min after plugging in. The cooling phase then converging to the ambient air temperature,
decreasing rapidly at first but then stabilizing as the heat dissipates. The cooling phase
is shown in Figure 4.4b.
4.2
Humidifier
A humidifier is a device that adds moisture to the air in order to maintain a desired level of
humidity. It is commonly used in incubators, greenhouses, and other environments where
humidity control is important for the growth and development of microorganisms. The
humidifier works by evaporating water and releasing water vapor into the air. By adding
moisture to the air, humidifiers help maintain optimal humidity levels for the growth of
microorganisms, preventing dehydration and promoting healthy growth. All humidifiers
should use distilled water to avoid mineral build-up and therefore minimize cleaning needs.
There are several types of humidifiers, including:
• Evaporative Humidifiers: These use a fan to blow air through a wet filter, evap-
orating water and adding humidity to the air.
They are self-regulating, as the
evaporation rate decreases as the humidity level increases.
• Ultrasonic Humidifiers: These use ultrasonic vibrations to create a fine mist of
water droplets that are released into the air. They are quiet and energy eﬀicient.
• Steam Vaporizers: These heat water to produce steam, which is then cooled
before being released into the air. They can be effective in increasing humidity, but
consume more energy than other types.
• Impeller Humidifiers: These use a rotating disk to fling water into a diffuser,
creating a fine mist. They are less common, but can be effective for larger spaces.
All humidifiers are suitable, but because of availability and price considerations, the ul-
trasonic humidifier is chosen. The ultrasonic humidifier is a compact and energy-eﬀicient
option that uses ultrasonic vibrations to create a fine mist of water droplets that are
released into the air.
This method is effective in maintaining humidity levels without
adding significant heat to the environment, which is important for temperature-sensitive
applications.
The humidifier is controlled by the main system with the measured humidity levels in
the enclosure and adjusts the humidifier output accordingly. The system uses a humidity
sensor to measure the relative humidity and turns the humidifier on or off as needed to
maintain the desired level.
4.2.1
Corrosion
Corrosion resistance is a critical consideration in the design and operation of heating
elements, particularly since the enclosure should be able to reach a relative high humidity
of 90%. The materials used in PTC element heaters are usually selected to minimize the
risk of corrosion and ensure long-term reliability.
Modular Incubation and Monitoring System for Sample Storage in High-Throughput Screening
35
==PAGE:44==
The PTC heating element used in the HTS machine is typically ceramics made of bar-
ium titanate (BaTiO￿). This material is chemically stable and is inherently resistant to
corrosion. Its ceramic nature ensures that it does not degrade or oxidize under normal
operating conditions, making it highly suitable for this humid environment.
The aluminum fins of the Peltier cooling unit are commonly used in PTC heaters to
improve heat dissipation. The fins are anodized to provide a protective oxide layer that
resists oxidation. However, prolonged exposure to high humidity or moisture can still lead
to the build-up of aluminum oxide, which may reduce the thermal eﬀiciency over time.
Regular maintenance and cleaning can mitigate this issue.
The enclosure plates are made of 304 stainless steel which is known for its excellent resis-
tance to corrosion, particularly in humid environments.
Electronic connections and solder joints are typically made with materials that are resistant
to corrosion, such as gold or nickel plating. These materials help prevent oxidation and
ensure reliable electrical connections over time, but can lead to shortages if too much
moisture is present.
Despite the inherent corrosion resistance of the materials used, certain environmental
factors can pose risks to the longevity and performance of the heating system:
• Humidity and High Temperatures: Prolonged exposure to water vapor in hu-
mid environments can accelerate the oxidation of metal components, particularly if
protective coatings are compromised.
• Salts or Contaminants: The presence of conductive particles, such as salts or
other contaminants, can lead to electrolytic corrosion. This is especially problematic
in environments where the heater is exposed to airborne particles or splashes.
• Electrical Contacts: Soldered or crimped electrical connections are particularly
susceptible to degradation over time if moisture is present. Corrosion at these points
can lead to increased resistance, overheating, or even failure of the heating element.
To minimize the risk of corrosion and ensure the longevity of the heating system, the
following solutions are recommended:
• Use of Distilled or Deionized Water: If the heater is used in conjunction with a
humidifier or exposed to water, distilled or deionized water should be used to prevent
mineral deposits and scaling on the heater’s surface.
• Protective Coatings: Ensure that all metal components, particularly aluminum
fins, are properly anodized or coated to resist oxidation.
• Sealed Electrical Connections: Use sealed or insulated connectors to protect
electrical contacts from moisture ingress.
• Regular Maintenance: Periodic inspection and cleaning of the heater and its
components can help identify and address early signs of corrosion or buildup.
Implementing these solutions might reduce the risk of corrosion, ensuring reliable operation
of the PTC heater and Peltier module in demanding humid environments.
4.2.2
Dehumidifier
A dehumidifier is a device that removes moisture from the air to maintain a desired level
of humidity and is mainly needed when the relative humidity of the ambient air is too
high for the application.
36
Modular Incubation and Monitoring System for Sample Storage in High-Throughput Screening
==PAGE:45==
This is rarely needed in incubators, as the relative humidity is usually kept at a high level
to ensure optimal growth conditions for microorganisms. However, in some cases, such as
when the ambient air is too humid or when the incubator is used for long-term storage of
samples, a dehumidifier may be necessary to prevent excess moisture build-up. Instead of
implementing additional components to achieve this, the Peltier module can be used to
cool the air and condense it to water vapor at the dew point, which is then collected in a
reservoir or drained away. The Peltier module can be used to cool the air to a temperature
below the dew point, causing the water vapor to condense and be removed from the air.
The dew point is defined by the temperature at which air becomes saturated with water
vapor and can no longer hold any more moisture. When air is cooled to this temperature,
the water vapor condenses into liquid water, which can then be collected or drained away.
The dew point is affected by the relative humidity and temperature of the air. The dew
point can be calculated using the following equation:
Td = T −(100 −RH)
5
(4.6)
where Td is the dew point temperature, T is the air temperature, and RH is the relative
humidity.
This equation is incorporated into the controlling system and its measured
humidity levels in the enclosure to adjust the output accordingly. The system uses multiple
sensors to measure relative humidity and turns the Peltier module on or off as needed to
maintain the desired level.
This is an effective way to remove moisture from the air, as it does not require any
additional components and can be used to cool the air meanwhile or the PTC heater can
be turned on to heat the air while the Peltier module reduces the relative humidity in the
air if necessary.
4.3
Climate unit
The heater, cooler and humidifier are integrated into a climate unit that is placed inside
the enclosure electronics compartment as seen in Figure 4.5a. Three climate units are
distributed along the base within the enclosure to ensure an even distribution of heat and
humidity throughout the enclosure.
(a) Assembled climate unit
(b) Open climate unit
Figure 4.5: Climate unit used in the enclosure.
Modular Incubation and Monitoring System for Sample Storage in High-Throughput Screening
37
==PAGE:46==
The climate unit is designed to be compact and easy to assemble, with all components
integrated into a single unit by 3D printed shells. It is mounted directly to the base plate
of the enclosure. From this, the Peltier module fits into a cutout hole in the bottom shell,
where it is fastened with two screws. A cutout in the module plate is made for the PTC
heater, while a fitted cutout in the shell is made to ensure a tight seal. The humidifier
is mounted within the climate unit in a hole where a small amount of distilled water is
stored. A section view of the climate unit is shown in Figure 4.6.
(a) Humidifier press fit geometry
(b) Snaps holding the two shells together
Figure 4.6: Section view of the climate unit
The stored water is evaporated by the ultrasonic humidifier mounted in the hole. A cavity
is made in the top shell to ensure that the large droplets that could get created by the
humidifier only fall back onto the piezo element and evaporate. In addition, the small
opening gap geometry only allows the humid air to escape, while the large water droplets
are kept inside the unit.
The small water storage can be filled manually or by using a small pump to fill it auto-
matically into one of the holes placed on the side. The other hole is used as a drain if too
much water is pumped into the small storage hole. If the water needs to be fully drained,
the pump can be reversed to pump out the water. As a backup, a small hole can be opened
to fully drain it, or the entire unit can be removed from the enclosure and emptied.
The climate unit is equipped with two snaps seen in Figure 4.6b and an overlapping
geometry that ensures ease of assembly and disassembly, while keeping a tight and secure
fit.
4.4
Air distribution
Air distribution is a critical aspect of the enclosure design, as it ensures that temperature
and humidity are evenly distributed throughout the enclosure. The air distribution system
is designed to provide uniform airflow and minimize dead zones, where stagnant air can
lead to uneven temperature and humidity levels.
The enclosure is designed to be a closed system, as this simplifies the design by not having
to worry about air exchange and filtering of the external air. When air is heated, it will
expand, creating a positive pressure difference compared to ambient air. This pressure
difference will cause air to slowly flow out of the small imperfections and gaps in the
enclosure, resulting in a natural air barrier. The air distribution system consists of three
main components:
38
Modular Incubation and Monitoring System for Sample Storage in High-Throughput Screening
==PAGE:47==
• Climate Units: The climate units are responsible for heating, cooling, and humid-
ifying the air in the enclosure. They are placed at the bottom of the enclosure to
ensure that air is heated or cooled before it rises to the top of the enclosure.
• Tangential Fans: The tangential fans are used to recirculate the air back down
to the climate unit and to create a convection current in the enclosure. They are
placed on the upper side panels of the enclosure.
• Air channels: The air channels are used to direct airflow from the top of the
enclosure to the climate units. They are designed to ensure that air will make a
laminar flow down to the electronic housing where the climate unit is placed.
Three climate units are evenly placed horizontally at the bottom of the enclosure. Hot
air is the main viable use case for the enclosure. This hot blown air from the bottom will
naturally rise to the top of the enclosure, where it will cool down and create a convection
current, which will help distribute the heat evenly throughout the enclosure. Furthermore,
the tangential fans placed at the top side panels of the enclosure will create a low pressure
corner, which will help draw the air from the bottom of the enclosure. The tangential fans
then blow it down back to the climate unit in the electronic housing. A tangential fan
is characterized by its ability to create a large pressure difference, which is ideal for this
application when air needs to flow through a long recirculation channel.
4.5
CFD simulation
Computational Fluid Dynamics (CFD) is a branch of fluid mechanics that uses numerical
analysis and algorithms to simulate and analyze the flow of fluids (liquids and gases)
and their interactions with solid surfaces.
CFD allows modeling of complex physical
phenomena such as airflow, heat transfer, turbulence, etc. by utilizing a numerical solution
of the Navier-Stokes equations.
At the core of CFD are a set of fundamental equations that govern fluid flow and heat
transfer. The most critical among these are the Navier-Stokes equations, which express
momentum conservation, the continuity equation, which ensures mass conservation, and
the energy equation, which describes heat transfer within the system.
The Navier-Stokes equations are a set of nonlinear partial differential equations that de-
scribe how the velocity field of a fluid evolves over time and space. They capture the
conservation of momentum in a fluid and are given by:
ρ
(∂u
∂t + u · ∇u
)
= −∇p + µ∇2u + F
(4.7)
where ρ is the fluid density, u is the velocity vector, p is pressure, µ is dynamic viscosity
and F represents body forces such as gravity. The left side of the equation represents the
total acceleration (rate of change of momentum) of a fluid element. The right side sums up
all the forces acting on the fluid: pressure forces, viscous (frictional) forces, and external
body forces.
The continuity equation ensures mass conservation and is expressed as: ∇·u = 0. It is the
divergence of the velocity field u, which describes how much fluid expands or compresses
at a point in space. It states that the divergence of the velocity field u is zero, meaning
that the amount of fluid entering any region is equal to the amount leaving, ensuring that
mass is conserved throughout the system. This equation is important for incompressible
flows, where the fluid density remains constant.
Modular Incubation and Monitoring System for Sample Storage in High-Throughput Screening
39
==PAGE:48==
The energy equation governs heat transfer and is written as:
ρcp
(∂T
∂t + u · ∇T
)
= k∇2T + Q
(4.8)
where cp is the specific heat capacity, T is the temperature, k is the thermal conductivity
and Q represents internal heat sources. This equation describes how temperature changes
within a fluid due to both convection and conduction, as well as any internal heat gen-
eration. The left side represents the rate of change of thermal energy in a fluid element,
combining local time-dependent and convective effects. The right side accounts for heat
conduction within the fluid, while Q includes any internal heat sources such as electrical
heating.
This equation is solved together with the continuity and momentum equations to predict
how temperature evolves and distributes throughout the air and solid regions. This allows
accurate modeling of heating, cooling, and temperature uniformity, which is essential for
maintaining the desired climate conditions inside the enclosure.
Together, these equations enable the calculation of velocity, pressure, and temperature
fields throughout the enclosure. By numerically solving them, CFD provides a detailed
understanding of airflow patterns, pressure distribution, and heat transfer. This insight is
crucial for optimizing the enclosure design, ensuring uniform temperature and humidity,
and accurately predicting the performance of the climate control system.
In CFD, the physical domain is discretized into a large number of small control volumes
or mesh cells (also called nodes or elements). At each node in the mesh, these governing
equations are transformed into algebraic equations using numerical methods such as the
finite volume, finite element, or finite difference method. The velocity, pressure, and tem-
perature variables are calculated at each node by iteratively solving these equations across
the entire mesh. The solution process involves updating the values at each node based on
the values of neighboring nodes, ensuring that the conservation laws are satisfied locally
and globally throughout the domain. This approach allows for the detailed prediction of
flow patterns, pressure fields, and temperature distributions, even in complex geometries.
The iterative solution continues until the residuals (the difference between the left and
right sides of the discretized equations) fall below a specified threshold, indicating that
the solution has converged. This node by node calculation is computationally intensive,
especially for large meshes, but provides highly detailed and accurate results for analysis
and design.[23]
CFD is widely used in engineering fields to optimize designs, predict performance, and
visualize fluid behavior in applications such as aerospace, automotive, HVAC, electronics
cooling, and biomedical devices. It provides detailed insights into velocity, pressure, tem-
perature, and other properties within a system, helping to improve eﬀiciency and solve
real-world problems without the need for extensive physical prototyping.
SimScale was used as the simulations tool, as it is able to connect to the cloud with
Onshape. The Conjugate Heat Transfer (IBM) method was used as it is a very robust
method to simulate flow and heat transfer in complex CAD assemblies. It solves the same
physics as Conjugate Heat Transfer v2.0 but immerses the CAD volumes in a cartesian
background grid IBM (Immersed Boundary Method) avoiding to resolve all parts in an
exact way during meshing. Therefore, it is resistant to undesired details that are insignifi-
cant for the overall thermal behavior. It is particularly suited for early stage design studies
on complex CAD assemblies like system-level thermal management, where the focus is on
overall heat transfer and airflow rather than fine geometric details.
40
Modular Incubation and Monitoring System for Sample Storage in High-Throughput Screening
==PAGE:49==
By using the Conjugate Heat Transfer method, SimScale enables rapid meshing and
simulation setup, even for large assemblies. This approach reduces the time required for
geometry preparation and mesh generation, allowing faster design iterations and early
identification of thermal or flow issues. As a result, it is possible to evaluate different
design concepts, optimize component placement, and assess the impact of design changes
on the overall climate performance of the enclosure.
4.5.1
CAD simplification
The CAD model includes small details which are not relevant for the simulation, such as
small holes, fillets, and other small details. Only the most important parts and subassem-
blies are carried over to SimScale, which is stated as the Original Model in Table 4.4 and
shown in Figure 4.7a. Meaning that the modules, power supplies, electronics and other
small details are not included in this model. (Modules could be included in future work)
The model is still complex and contains small details such as the profile geometry of the
aluminum extrusions and the small holes in the enclosure. This adds faces and edges to
the model, which could result in singularities or make it harder to solve. Singularities
are often a problem when CAD models have a lot of small details with sharp corners or
irregular surfaces, as they can cause numerical errors and convergence issues during the
simulation. This leads to the mesh needing to be very fine to comprehend the small details,
which leads to long simulation time and a lot of memory usage.
To solve this problem, the model was simplified by remaking the whole CAD model with
only the most important details. Tolerances and small fillets were removed and the ge-
ometry was simplified to only include the walls with insulation and the air channel. This
simplification is shown as the Simplified Model in Table 4.4.
Model
Faces
Volumes
Edges
Face Reduction
Original Model
11692
154
30187
-
Simplified Model
881
38
2055
92%
Simplified Symmetry Model
399
26
959
97%
Table 4.4: Comparison of model simplifications for the simulation
A symmetry model was also created, where the model was cut in half and only one
side of the model was used. This was used because the geometry and the expected flow
field pattern of the developed solution are mirrored along that surface. By using this
boundary condition, the domain is essentially halved, reducing the time required to achieve
a solution. This is possible because the model and the airflow are symmetric and follow
the following assumptions:
• The fluxes across the symmetry are zero.
• The normal components of all variables are set to zero.
• The geometry and boundary conditions are mirrored across the symmetry plane.
In detail, the symmetry condition applies the constraints on the flow variables, resulting in
a halved domain. Specifically, at the symmetry plane, the normal component of velocity
and the normal gradients of all scalar quantities (such as pressure and temperature) are set
to zero. This means that there is no mass, momentum, or energy flux across the symmetry
boundary, effectively mirroring the solution on either side of the plane. By enforcing these
constraints, the simulation assumes that the physical behavior on one side of the plane
is identical to that on the other, which is valid when both the geometry and boundary
conditions are symmetric.
Modular Incubation and Monitoring System for Sample Storage in High-Throughput Screening
41
==PAGE:50==
Utilizing a symmetry boundary condition offers significant computational advantages. By
reducing the size of the computational domain by half, the number of mesh elements
and, consequently, the computational resources and simulation time required are greatly
decreased.
This allows for faster design iterations and enables higher mesh resolution
within the same computational budget, improving the accuracy of the results. The three
CAD models shown in Table 4.4 are shown in Figure 4.7.
(a) Original Model
(b) Simplified Model
(c) Sym. Model
Figure 4.7: Iterative CAD model simplification used for the CFD simulations
Volume allocations
Each volume in the model is assigned the corresponding material properties used in the
simulation. This means that every distinct volume is defined with its own set of ther-
mal and physical characteristics. Assigning accurate material properties is important for
obtaining realistic simulation results, as these properties directly influence how heat is
conducted, stored, and transferred within the system.
Air volumes are treated as compressible fluids, allowing the simulation to account for
changes in air density and heat capacity with temperature and pressure. By allocating
these properties to each volume, the simulation can accurately capture the complex inter-
actions between solid and fluid regions, predict temperature gradients, and evaluate the
effectiveness of the enclosure’s insulation and air temperature uniformity. The specific
materials and their properties are listed in Table 4.5.

as a compressible fluid. Air volumes were created to fit the proper dimensions of the air
channel, climate unit and enclosure volume, as seen in Figure 4.8a. To evaluate the impact
of the insulation from the temperature distribution, an external air volume was added to
represent the ambient air surrounding the enclosure. The external air volume extends
approximately 3 meters beyond the enclosure in every direction except the bottom, as the
enclosure is positioned on a table.

4.5.2
Boundary conditions
The ambient air volume has wall boundary condition on all sides, except the bottom
and symmetry plane.
The wall condition is set to a temperature of 21.5°C, which is
the measured temperature in the physical room in which the enclosure is placed, and is
therefore the most representative and comparable temperature for the ambient air.
The PTC heater has a maximum power of 200W, while the highest most common tem-
perature is 37°C, to which the heaters are set. The bottom axial fans are mounted to
the PTC heater and have a large volumetric flow rate of 0.01 m3/s and a fan pressure of
60Pa. The upper tangential fans are mounted on the inner side of the enclosure and have
a smaller volumetric flow rate of 0.0071 m3/s, but a higher fan pressure of 199.3Pa.
A symmetry boundary condition is applied to the plane of symmetry, where the given
heating and volumetric flow rates are halved. This is done to ensure that the flow and
temperature distribution is symmetric across the plane. The boundary conditions for the
CFD simulation are summarized in Table 4.6.

4.5.3
Mesh visualization
Multiple iterations of the mesh were made to ensure a good quality mesh. The mesh
was created using SimScale and its standard sizing algorithm set to automatic with a
finess of 2. Automatic boundary layers were used to ensure good quality mesh around the
walls. Physics-based meshing was also enabled to take the setup information like materials,
boundary conditions, and source terms into account to size the mesh accordingly. This
adaption allows for refinements on inlets and outlets and different sizing for solid and
Modular Incubation and Monitoring System for Sample Storage in High-Throughput Screening
43
==PAGE:52==
fluid regions in conjugated heat transfer (CHT) simulations. Hex element core was also
enabled, which changes the core of the volumes to hexahedral elements (with transition of
tetrahedral and pyramid elements), while keeping the outer layers as tetrahedral elements
to fit the geometries. This is done to enable higher accuracy and more eﬀicient computation
for the same number of degrees of freedom compared to tetrahedral elements, when solving
the flow and heat transfer equations. The mesh is shown in Figure 4.9,
(a) Whole model (right view)
(b) Enclousure
(c) Enclosed air
Figure 4.9: Mesh of the CFD model
4.5.4
Residuals
Residuals represent the error between the left- and right-hand sides of an equation dur-
ing an iterative numerical solution. They quantify how well the governing equations are
satisfied at each iteration. Residuals are used to monitor the convergence of the solution,
with lower residuals indicating a more accurate solution.
Appendix A.2 shows the residuals from the following shown simulation results. The resid-
uals are shown as a function of the number of iterations, with the x-axis representing the
number of iterations and the y-axis representing the residual value. The most important
residuals are the temperature, as this is the main variable of interest in these simulations.
The residual temperature converges to a value just below 10−3, which is suﬀicient for
these estimations. A lower residual value indicates a more accurate and converged solu-
tion. However, achieving this typically requires significantly longer simulation times. This
is possible to achieve by increasing the number of iterations and further refining the mesh,
but this was not prioritized because of the suﬀicient results gathered and time constraints.
4.5.5
Airflow distribution
This simulation is made to estimate how well the air flows in the enclosure and how
well the air is distributed throughout the enclosure. This is directly correlated with the
temperature distribution and contamination in the enclosure. The simulation uses the
SimScale advanced concepts of momentum sources and power sources.
In CFD, a momentum source is a numerical tool used to represent the effect of a fan or
blower within a closed system, by injecting momentum into the flow without requiring a
physical inlet or outlet. Similarly, a power source can be used to simulate the heat input
from PTC heaters. These approaches are particularly useful for modeling recirculating
systems such as this enclosure, where air is continuously circulated rather than exchanged
with the environment.
By utilizing these concepts, the flow and velocity magnitudes can be estimated. The mo-
mentum source is used to simulate the effect of the fans and is defined by their properties.
It considers the direction, volumetric flow rate ( ˙V ) and fan pressure (∆p) which are de-
fined in Table 4.6. To better understand airflow patterns, a particle trace visualization is
44
Modular Incubation and Monitoring System for Sample Storage in High-Throughput Screening
==PAGE:53==
used. This technique releases virtual particles into the simulated airflow, allowing us to
observe how air moves throughout the enclosure, identify recirculation zones, and assess
whether there are stagnant areas or uneven distribution.
Figure 4.10 shows the resulting particle traces from the center heater as purple dots. The
visualization highlights how air is distributed by fans, indicating the effectiveness of the
air distribution system and helping to identify any potential areas where airflow may be
insuﬀicient.
Figure 4.10: Particle trace of the airflow in the enclosure
The purple dots in the center heater represents the particle seedpoints, which are followed
by the colored lines that show the airflow in the enclosure. The figure shows how air flows
through the heater with high velocities around 4m/s, which then slowly rises to the top of
the enclosure. Some of the traces act turbulent at low velocities, while others go in a more
laminar but slow flow up and out to the recirculation blower fan on the side wall. The
blown air is then recirculated with high velocities to the bottom of the enclosure, where
it is heated and pushed out again by the PTC heater fan.
The low velocity magnitude of the air in the enclosure ensures that the temperature
distribution is even, with no hot spots except for the bottom, while also ensuring that
the air is not blown too hard, which could lead to contamination of the samples in the
enclosure. If air is blown too hard, it could cause the sample lids to blow off, samples
being disturbed, or large temperature differences because of the upward flow direction it
creates. This configuration creates a recirculating airflow in the enclosure, which helps
distribute heat evenly throughout the enclosure. Note that Figure 4.10 only shows the
flow from the center fan, but the same flow pattern is seen for the other two fans as well.
Modular Incubation and Monitoring System for Sample Storage in High-Throughput Screening
45
==PAGE:54==
4.5.6
Temperature distribution
A steady-state Conjugate Heat Transfer (CHT) simulation has been created to estimate
the temperature distribution and the overall difference in the enclosure. CHT simulations
are used to model both the heat transfer within solid components (such as walls and
insulation) and the heat exchange with the surrounding ambient air, providing a realistic
picture of how temperature is distributed throughout the system.
The heater temperature is set to 30 °C and is also simulated at 37 °C, which is the most
common worst-case temperature used in the enclosure. While the PTC heaters are not
directly temperature adjustable, it is assumed that a Proportional Integral Derivative
(PID) feedback loop from the temperature sensors in the enclosure can maintain this fixed
temperature.
The simulation is set to steady-state, meaning that it calculates the final temperature dis-
tribution after the system has reached equilibrium, rather than modeling how temperatures
change over time, which can lead to long simulation times and increased computational
resources. This approach is useful for understanding the long-term thermal behavior of
the enclosure under constant operating conditions.
Compressibility is enabled in the simulation to account for changes in air density due to
temperature and pressure variations, which can be significant near heating elements and
blower fans. This approach is more accurate than the Boussinesq approximation, which
assumes small density changes and is only valid for small temperature differences. The
simulation uses the k-omega SST turbulence model, which is well suited for capturing the
effects of turbulence near walls and in regions with adverse pressure gradients. This model
provides a good balance between accuracy and computational eﬀiciency, making it ideal
for simulating both recirculating airflow and heat transfer in the enclosure.
Figure 4.11: Section view of diffusers temperature gradient effect.
Initially, simulation tests were run to indicate any aggressive temperature gradients that
could be fixed with additional components or fans.
A slight temperature decrease in
temperature uniformity was identified in the top row compared to the bottom row, as the
46
Modular Incubation and Monitoring System for Sample Storage in High-Throughput Screening
==PAGE:55==
section plate creates a small barrier for the airflow. This was identified and fixed with
three small diffusers placed in front of the bottom fans, as shown in Figure 4.11.
The diffusers are designed to distribute the airflow more evenly throughout the enclosure,
using an angle of approximately 78° degrees to direct the airflow towards the top of the
enclosure.
This angle is determined from the angles shown in Figure 4.11, where the
surface of the diffusers has the corresponding angle to the airflow direction of around 51°
degrees, instead of blowing the air straight up at 45° degrees or not having any diffuser.
Additionally, the sides are angled, enhancing the distribution between the heater outlets.
Figure 4.11 shows that the addition of diffusers results in an approximate 0.5 °C increase
in the top row temperature, thus improving the overall temperature uniformity within the
enclosure. Consequently, diffusers are incorporated into the final design and used within
the enclosure throughout this report.
The final simulation results, shown in Figure 4.12 and Figure 4.13, illustrate the temper-
ature distribution throughout the enclosure. These figures help identify any temperature
gradients, hot or cold spots, and assess whether the heating system provides uniform
conditions as required.
Figure 4.12: Temperature distribution of the enclosure at steady-state 30 °C
The external temperature distribution of the enclosure reveals that the aluminum extru-
sions are the hottest areas, serving as the primary heat transfer path to the surrounding
air. This is expected, given the high thermal conductivity of aluminum at 235W/(m·K)
(see Chapter 3). The front bottom sections are particularly warm, as this is where the
PTC heaters expel hot air. In contrast, the enclosure walls demonstrate effective insu-
lation, with only a small temperature increase compared to ambient conditions. This is
especially evident on the backplate and front door windows in Figure 4.12, which show a
slight temperature rise of about 4°C above room temperature.
Modular Incubation and Monitoring System for Sample Storage in High-Throughput Screening
47
==PAGE:56==
In order to get a good view of the temperature distribution inside the enclosure, two
section views are made at the center of the enclosure of the front and side, which is shown
in Figure 4.13. Note that the front section is still the half of the enclosure (using symmetry
assumptions), which has been mirrored to show the temperature distribution of the whole
enclosure.
Figure 4.13: Sections of the temperature distribution of the enclosure 30 °C input
The figure demonstrates that the temperature distribution within the enclosure is quite
uniform, with values just above 29°C. Slightly higher temperatures are observed in the
center of the front section, as the three heaters are set to a constant 30°C, which causes air
to be marginally warmer where wall contact is minimal. In practice, this minor difference
will be corrected by a PID controller, which uses feedback from temperature sensors to
adjust heater output, reducing power to the center heater as needed. The section plate in
the middle introduces a small gradient, resulting in slightly more uniform temperatures in
the middle of the bottom row compared to the top.
Overall, Figure 4.13 confirms that the enclosure maintains an even temperature distribu-
tion. To achieve a target temperature of 30°C, the heaters must be set almost one degree
higher, compensating for heat loss through the enclosure walls. This difference increases
at higher setpoints, e.g. at 37°C, the required heater output is estimated to be 1–2°C
above the desired enclosure temperature (see Appendix A).
An additional simulation was performed with the heater set to a more realistic 30.6°C.
Based on the previous 30°C simulation, it was determined that the heater should be 0.6°C
warmer to achieve an enclosure temperature of approximately 30°C, a correction the PID
controller would make in practice. While SimScale provides average temperature values,
it does not calculate standard deviations. To address this, 24 probe points were evenly
distributed across the section to determine both the average temperature and standard
deviation. The locations and results of these probe points are detailed in Appendix A.1.3
and will be referenced in the evaluation of temperature distribution in Section 8.7.

5
Modular Framework
Modern laboratory and industrial environments increasingly require systems that are flexi-
ble and easily adaptable to evolving user requirements. The modular framework addresses
this need by providing a versatile enclosure system composed of interchangeable modules.
These modules can be arranged in two rows of five, as illustrated in Figure 2.2, allowing
users to tailor the system’s functionality by simply adding or removing modules according
to specific application needs.
This modular approach not only streamlines customization and future upgrades, but also
ensures that the system remains user-friendly, quick to assemble or reconfigure, and me-
chanically robust. Each module is engineered for straightforward integration, featuring a
secure yet easily detachable connection mechanism.
A standard module consists of nine key components: bottom and top structural parts,
an external shell, a pogo pin mount, pogo pins for electrical connectivity, a T-track for
mechanical fastening, left and right snap-on lids, a cable cap, and eight countersunk M4x12
stainless steel machine screws.
This construction has strength, ease of assembly, and
adaptability, forming the foundation for a modular and expandable enclosure system.
(a) Barebone module
(b) Back view
(c) Top and bottom
(d) Exploded view
Figure 5.1: Barebone module and its different views.
The plastic parts have been 3D printed from Polylactic Acid (PLA), while the pogo pin
mount and cable cap are 3D printed using Thermoplastic Polyurethane (TPU), to give
the parts flexibility.
The bottom part has multiple features as shown in Figure 5.2. Two electronic compart-
ments have been made to house a microcontroller or other electronic components. The
compartments are designed to be easy to access with removable snap-on lids. In the mid-
dle of the compartments the T-track is mounted with a small recess for ball plungers,
which is used to securely fasten the module to the enclosure securely. Angled guides are
incorporated at the track inlet to facilitate easier installation.
The top part has the same overall dimensions as the bottom part, but is simplified to
only have track guidance and a small recess for the ball plungers, so no track needs to be
Modular Incubation and Monitoring System for Sample Storage in High-Throughput Screening
49
==PAGE:58==
screwed in place. When the bottom and top part are screwed together with the shell, it
creates a very strong and rigid module.

The shell is made of 304 stainless steel with a thickness of 2mm. It is designed to be easy
to clean and maintain, with a smooth surface, while being easy to manufacture. It is made
on a laser cutter, where the holes are chamfered for countersunk M4 screws. Lastly, it is
bent twice to create the surrounding rigid shell, which ensures good structural integrity.
The drawing sent to the tool shop is shown in Appendix E.2.
The pogo pin assembly is mounted with an interference fit onto the 3D printed pogo pin
mount, which is installed in-between the bottom part and the shell. The mount is made of
flexible TPU and aligned to the bottom part with small flexible features as seen in Figure
5.2. This flexibility allows for a better fit to the female connection mounted on the back
plate of the enclosure. By doing so, the assembly is more forgiving to misalignment and
large tolerances between the module and back plate, as it will self-align using the pogo
pin magnets.

modules are not limited to the ideas shown, but can be anything that fits the incubation

All modules are based on the same barebone module, which serves as a standardized

The modular approach allows for flexibility in both design and application. For example,
the Dot module can be used for mounting sensors or actuators, while the Storage module
provides space for storing sample plates.

for future developments.
5.1.1
Dot module
The Dot module does not have a function in itself, but can be used as a development
module as the array of 3.3mm holes can be used to mount any type of sensor or device.
The holes diameter of 3.3mm was chosen as it can be used for M3 machine screws and nuts
with a small clearance, while having the desired diameter for threading for M4 screws. The
holes are placed in a 16x9 grid with 25mm equal spacing everywhere to allow flexibility in
the developing process. The dimensions of the dot plate are shown in Figure 5.6.
425mm

25mm

Figure 5.6: Dot plate dimensions
5.1.2
Present plate module
The Present plate module enables external robots to collect and deliver sample plates
for incubation in the enclosure, facilitating fully automated operation of the incubation
system. This module acts as an interface between the robotic handling system and the
incubator, allowing for seamless transfer of plates without manual intervention.
The present plate module is designed on top of the dot plate, with two simple 3D printed
parts. A base part attaches to the dot module using M3 screws and nuts, while having a
cutout for the servo motor. The servo motor rotates the sample plate 90 degrees to the
front or side of the enclosure. A hole is made in the enclosure to allow an external robot to
pick up or deliver sample plates or place more incubation systems side by side, expanding
its sample throughput and functionality.
5.1.3
Storage module
The Storage module is designed to hold and store sample plates, such as 96-well mi-
croplates, 96-deepwell microplates and petri dishes in a compact and organized form fac-
tor. The storage module uses a plate with squared holes that allow for positioning and
snapping of the storage plate holders. The storage module is designed to hold 26 storage
plates with 13 in each of the two columns. Using this design, any plate holder with the
correct snap feature can be plugged into place, adding flexibility to the desired sample
plate system. A 96-well microplate holder has been developed and manufactured, which
is shown in Figure 5.7b.

The storage module and its plate holder is designed with two snap hooks that lock in

the insert can be made of multiple materials, including brittle materials, such as PLA,

developed with precise tolerances at certain dimensions, which worked best with Prusa 3D
printers. Different printers showed unreliable tolerance results, which is why only premium
and reliable printers will be used in the future. The kinematics of the snap and positioning

Through testing and multiple prints, it was shown that the plate holder was hard to detach
when all plates were in place. The plates are packed so closely that it is hard to get a hand
in and press on the snaps to detach them. A second iteration of the plate holder was made
to make this easier, which is shown in Figure 5.9a. The second iteration has a pivoting
snap feature, which allows the user to press on the snap feature further out, making it
easier to detach the plate holder. The plateholder is made of Polyethylene Terephthalate
Glycol (PETG) to ensure that the material is flexible enough to allow the snap feature to
pivot, and it is also much less brittle compared to PLA. Additionally, a small petri dish
cutout has been made, so the plate can handle multiple types of sample plates.

W = P ·
µ + tan(α)

(5.1)
Where P is the deflection force, µ is the friction coeﬀicient against steel, and α is the
hook inclination angle which is tan(42◦) ≈0.90. The deflection force P is found using a
static deflection simulation of the snap-fit geometry, which is shown in Figure 5.9b and in
Appendix B. The plate is fixed everywhere except the snap hook, where a displacement
of 1.2mm is applied, which is the designed required deflection of the hook. The deflection
force P is then measured at the applied deflection and found to be:
PPLA ≈3.86 N
PPETG ≈2.33 N

Two snap hooks are used to hold the plate holder in place, which means that the total
mating force is doubled:
WTotal, PLA = 2 · 7.04N ≈14.08 N
WTotal, PETG = 2 · 4.73N ≈9.46 N
(5.5)
The mating force W is the force needed to push the snap hooks into the plate holder,
which is relatively low and easy to do by hand. The PETG is more flexible and requires
less force to snap into place, while PLA is more rigid and requires more force. The mating
force is well within the limits of the materials used, as stress plots from the static deflection
simulation (Figure 5.9b and Appendix B) show that the maximum stress in the snap hooks
is below the yield strength of both materials, which means that the snap hooks will not
break or plastically deform during mounting and operation.
A strain evaluation at the root of the snap-fit following the Bayer snap-fit design guide[24]
is performed to ensure that the snap-fit can withstand repeated use without failure. The
maximum strain ε at the root of a tapered cantilever snap-fit is calculated using the
formula:
ε =
y · h
1.09 · l2 = 1.2mm · 3.5mm
1.09 · (33mm)2 ≈0.00354 = 0.354%
(5.6)
This strain is significantly below the typical safe strain limits for repeated snap-fit use:
εsafe, PLA = 0.036 = 3.6%
εsafe, PETG = 0.048 = 4.8%
(5.7)
The snap-fit geometry is mechanically safe for both PLA and PETG, with strain levels
well within the allowable limits for repeated use. Both are suﬀicient while PETG is more
flexible and less brittle, making it a better choice for the snap-fit design. These results
show that the design is suitable for repeated use and will not fail during operation.

All solutions in Figure 5.10 might work well, but each have their own advantages and
disadvantages. To narrow down the options and choose a connection method, the following
criteria were set:
• Ease of use: The connection should be intuitive and not require special tools.
• Speed: It should allow for quick connection and disconnection.
• Secure: The connection must remain stable and not loosen during operation.
• Size: The interface should occupy minimal space within the system.
• Cleaning: It should be easy to clean and require little to no maintenance.
• Cost: It should be cost-effective and not significantly increase the system cost.
These criteria apply to both the electronic and mechanical connections. Although they
function together, each should be evaluated independently, as all electronic and mechanical
connections are compatible.

Table 5.2: Scoring matrix for electronic connections (all criteria 1-5 scale, with total).
Total score is color-coded: green (best), yellow (medium and lowest).
Pogo pins and the Universal Serial Bus (USB) interfaces scored the highest, with pogo
pins being the best option. Pogo pins are a type of spring-loaded connector that securely
attaches to a flat pin interface with magnets, making a secure and reliable connection
between two modules. They are easy to use, fast to connect and disconnect, and have a
small form factor. Pogo pins are also easy to clean and maintain, making them a good
choice for the modular framework.
The USB connection and especially USB-C was considered a lot because of its popularity,
ease of use, speed, and the fact that it is a well-known adopted standard that EU regula-
tions have aligned with.[25] However, it is not as easy to clean and the alignment of the
interface is very important for USB-C in order to connect, while Pogo pins have magnets
that help align the connection. Based on Table 5.2 and these arguments, pogo pins were
chosen as the connection method for the modular framework.
Pogo pin protocol
Pogo pins allow custom protocols, as they can be set in a array of multiple pins that can be
used for power and multiple data lines. Multiple protocols can be used, such as USB, Inter-
56
Modular Incubation and Monitoring System for Sample Storage in High-Throughput Screening
==PAGE:65==
Integrated Circuit (I2C), SPI and UART. These protocols are all serial communication
protocols, which means that they send and receive data one bit at a time.
The pogo pin protocol criteria were set to ensure that most use cases of modules are
covered. The criteria are as follows:
• Speed: It should be future proof and be able to transmit 4k video at 60Hz, which
is 15-100 MB/s depending on the compression used.
• Distance: It should be able to transmit data at a distance of 2 meters.
• Power: It should be able to power devices of at least 2W at a voltage of 5V.
• Use case: It should be able to connect to a wide range of devices and sensors, such
as cameras, displays, and other peripherals.
In order to determine the number of pins needed for the protocols, a list of the most
common protocols used with pogo pins was created. The list is shown in Table 5.3.

Table 5.3: Comparison of common protocols used with pogo pins.
The table shows the most common protocols used with pogo pins, along with the number
of pins needed, speed, distance, power, cost, and use case. The table is not complete and
only shows a few examples of the most common protocols. Speed, distance, and power are
approximate values and can vary depending on the specific implementation and use case.
Note that power for I2C and SPI should be supplied through separate Vcc/GND lines, not
the protocol itself, and would therefore add 2 more pins to the pogo pin connection. Where
the USB protocols have integrated power lines in the protocol. The I2C and SPI protocols
have a speed range as they are not fully standardized and depend on microcontroller
capabilities, which are often low speed and low power as they are used primarily for
sensors.
The most common protocol used with pogo pins is I2C, which is a two-wire protocol that
uses a clock and a data line to communicate between devices. I2C is a popular choice to
connect sensors and other peripherals to microcontrollers, as it is easy to use and requires
only two wires.
SPI is another common protocol used with pogo pins, which is a four-wire protocol that
uses a clock, data in, data out, and chip select line to communicate between devices. SPI
is faster than I2C, but requires more wires and is more complex to use.
USB 2.0 and USB 3.0 are also common protocols used with pogo pins, which are high-
speed protocols that can transfer data at speeds of up to 480Mbps and 5Gbps, respectively.
USB is a popular choice to connect peripherals to computers and other devices, as it is
easy to use and widely supported.
1Power must be supplied via separate Vcc/GND lines.
Modular Incubation and Monitoring System for Sample Storage in High-Throughput Screening
57
==PAGE:66==
The criteria stated earlier were compared to the protocols in Table 5.3, where USB 2.0
and USB 3.0 are the best options for the pogo pin protocol. USB 2.0 is a good choice
for most use cases, as it is fast, easy to use, only 4 pins, and widely supported. USB 3.0
is a better choice for high-speed applications as it is 10 times faster but is more complex
and requires 9 pins. Based on the criteria and comparison of the protocols, USB 3.0 was
chosen for the higher speed and power rating.
USB 3.0 is an expansion of USB 2.0 and is backward compatible, which means it can
be used with USB 2.0 devices. This makes it a good choice for the modular framework,
as it allows for easy integration with only 4 wires for most use cases and the total of 9
pins for the more demanding high-speed modules. The USB 3.0 protocol is also widely
supported and has a large ecosystem of available devices and peripherals, which makes it
a good choice for the modular framework. The pogo pin connection and wiring are shown
in Figure 5.11.
(a) Pogo pin wiring.
(b) Male and female pogo pin as-
semblies without wires.
Figure 5.11: Pogo pin connection and wiring.
The pogo pin connection assembly consists of 9 pogo pins and two opposite mounted
magnets. The magnets are used to align the connection and make it easier to connect and
disconnect the modules, while ensuring that the connections are not flipped. The male
pogo pin assembly is mounted on the modules, while the flat female pogo pin assembly is
mounted on the inner back wall of the enclosure. This makes it easy to clean and maintain,
as there are no visible wires or dents in the wall.
The first 4 pins are the USB 2.0 pins, which are used for data transfer and power. The
next 5 pins are the expansion to USB 3.0, which are used for high-speed data transfer,
power, and common ground. Only the first 4 pins are needed for basic connections, where
the rest can remain unused in the module itself. If the module is a camera, display, or
other high-speed device, the extra pins can be used to connect within the module and
allow data transfers up to 625MB/s.
5.2.2
Fastening
While the connection provides electronic communication and power, the fastening provides
the mechanical connection between to the modules. The criteria are the same as stated
earlier: ease of use, speed, secure, size, cleaning, and cost. This is compared between the
fastening methods proposed in Figure 5.10, but it is diﬀicult to compare quantitatively, as
the fastening methods are not standardized and depend on the specific implementation.
Some of the fastening methods also depend on each other and are not mutually exclusive,
such as the pogo pins and magnets for the electronic connection.
After brainstorming on different fastening methods and making market research for avail-
able methods, the T-slot fastening method was chosen. The T-slot fastening method is a
58
Modular Incubation and Monitoring System for Sample Storage in High-Throughput Screening
==PAGE:67==
modular fastening system that uses a T-shaped slot to connect two modules together. It
is widely used in the woodworking and construction industry as machine guides, such as
manual sawing machines.
The T-slot fastening method is easy to use, fast to connect and disconnect, and provides
a secure connection between the modules. The T-slot fastening method is also small and
does not take up too much space in the system. Its fastening method is easy to clean and
maintain even though its popularity is mainly anodized aluminum, which can be sensitive
to strong cleaning agents if the surface is damaged. Stainless steel solutions might also be
available, but were not found at a reasonable price in market research.
The tracks have been extruded and anodized, making them inexpensive and easy to pro-
duce. They have then been cut to size and countersunk fastening holes have been drilled.
The male and female T-track is shown in Figure 5.12.
(a) Male T-track
(b) Female T-track
Figure 5.12: Ordered T-track profiles
The tracks shown in Figure 5.12 are raw tracks that were cut to size and drilled. The
male T-track is mounted on the module plate and section plate in the enclosure, while
the female T-track is fitted to the modules itself, ensuring a clean look with few visible
geometries. To ensure a good fit and reduce machining of T-tracks, a small spacer was
3D printed to ensure that the modules height is aligned correctly with the feet of them,
as the male track was not high enough from the manufacture to fit properly. The module
is fastened using the T-tracks, while minimizing overconstrains as shown in Figure 5.13.
Figure 5.13: Section view of module fastening 2D kinematics
The track pulls the module down with a small interference fit, ensuring that the feet stay
on the module base. This creates a small attachment force so that the module does not
rattle during operation. The ball plungers add a small z force to the module, keeping it
at the inserted position. In addition to the kinematics shown in Figure 5.13, the module
Modular Incubation and Monitoring System for Sample Storage in High-Throughput Screening
59
==PAGE:68==
also has an additional Rz lock and a ball plunger in the top part. This was done to ensure
that the module aligning correctly and did not rattle. However, after manufacturing and
testing the module, it was found that the module was very rigid and did not rattle at all.
The top track is therefore not needed and can be removed in the future.
Ball plunger force calculation
To ensure that the modules are held securely in place while still allowing for easy removal,
the required ball plunger force was calculated based on ergonomic and mechanical con-
siderations. The calculation follows the spring plunger force formula from the Wixroyd
datasheet [26]:
Fx =
F
tan(α/2)
(5.8)
where F is the spring force of the plunger, Fx is the pull or push force required to release
the plunger, and α is the angle of the indexing counterpart face.
Figure 5.14:
Ball plunger
force diagram and geometry
with symbols.[26]
Given two M4 plungers (top and bottom track), each with
a spring force F = 20 N, and an indexing angle α = 120°:
F = 2 · 20 N = 40 N
α = 120° = 2
3π ≈2.09 rad
Fx =
F
tan(α/2) =
40 N
tan(60°) ≈23.09 N
Friction forces from the feet are added:
m = 5 kg
g = 9.81 m/s2
FN = m · g = 5 kg · 9.81 m/s2 = 49.05 N
Ffriction = µsteel,PLA · FN = 0.25 · 49.05 N = 12.26 N
Ftotal, pull = Fx + Ffriction = 23.09 N + 12.26 N = 35.35 N
This pull force is quit high and equal to a pull of around 3.5 kg, but should be comfortable
for most users according to ergonomic studies. The maximum ergonomic pulling force is
estimated to be 10% the weight of a 50 kg person resulting in a maximum pulling force of
around 50 N, which is well above the calculated required pulling force of 35.35 N.
To improve the ergonomics of module handling, the weight can be reduced by using thinner
shells or incorporating cutouts, while the required pull force can be further reduced by
removing one of the ball plungers or adjusting the indexing angle. Consequently, the top
ball plunger was omitted, lowering the total pull force to approximately 23.81, N. [27]
Overall, the developed modular framework addresses the need for flexibility, ease of use,
and robust performance in laboratory and industrial environments. By standardizing both
the mechanical and electrical interfaces, the system enables rapid prototyping, straight-
forward customization, and reliable operation. The use of T-slot fastening and pogo pin
connections ensures that modules can be quickly swapped or upgraded without compromis-
ing structural integrity or connectivity. This modular framework lays a solid foundation
for future development, supporting a wide range of modules and use cases. Its adaptability
and scalability will facilitate ongoing innovation, making it a valuable platform for current
and future laboratory automation needs.

Modular Incubation and Monitoring System for Sample Storage in High-Throughput Screening
==PAGE:69==
6
Electronics and Software
The electronics and software for the machine need to control multiple components, includ-
ing heating, cooling, humidifier, light, various modules, etc., and the future implementa-
tion of the robotic arm and gripper. A computer that can both run the user interface
and control the hardware is needed with multiple USB and General Purpose Input/Out-
put (GPIO) pins. The Raspberry Pi 5 is chosen as the main computer for the machine, as
it has a powerful quad-core processor, multiple USB ports and GPIO pins for controlling
hardware. Its a SoC (System on Chip) computer, which means that it has all the necessary
components integrated into a single chip, making it compact and eﬀicient. The Raspberry
Pi 5 is capable of running a full operating system, such as Linux. An overview of all the
electronics that the Raspberry control is shown in Figure 6.1.
Figure 6.1: Electronics overview
The BTT (BigTreeTech) board is a 3D printer controller board that is used to precisely
control the motors using G-code commands. This is used to control the robotic arm and
gripper and was developed by the previous HTS project[2]. The Raspberry Pi 5 has the
capabilities to run this BTT board by sending G-code commands through a USB connec-
tion, while controlling the rest of the hardware through GPIO pins and USB connections,
which is then shown on a touch-friendly user interface. All of this can be handled by the
Python programming language, which is used to develop the user interface and control
the hardware. The user interface is developed using the Kivy library, which allows for the
creation of multitouch applications. The Python language mainly runs line by line, but
with implementations of threading it can handle multiple tasks at the same time, such as
reading sensors, controlling hardware, and updating the user interface.
6.1
Wiring diagram
All components are controlled with a MOSFET, a high-capacity transistor, to control the
flow of power based on a PWM signal. This allows for precise control of the voltage or
current to a load, essentially creating a variable power supply. Three dedicated power
supplies are used to provide the required voltage levels for the system: 5V, 12V, and 24V.
The 5V supply powers the Raspberry Pi, touchscreen, and controllers. The 12V supply is
dedicated to the Peltier modules and associated fans. The 24V supply provides power for
Modular Incubation and Monitoring System for Sample Storage in High-Throughput Screening
61
==PAGE:70==
the PTC heaters, as well as additional fans and lighting. The three power supplies were
rated at different current and power levels, which is shown in Table 6.1.

6.1.1
Climate unit wiring
The Raspberry Pi has two GPIO’s capable of hardware PWM signaling, and there is a total
of 26 GPIO’s available for software PWM. However, the CPU gets overloaded when using
software PWM and it can be unstable depending on the load on the processor. Therefore,
a PCA9685 controls the PWM signals through hardware using I2C communication. It
is a 16-channel PWM controller that can be used to control multiple devices with PWM
signals. The PWM signals provide controllable power in a duty cycle of 0% to 100%,
resulting in a output of 0V to 3.3V. The voltage and current output from the PWM
signal is low, so a MOSFET with an optocoupler is used to control the power to all
components. A MOSFET is a type of transistor that can be used to switch high voltages
and currents on and off. An optocoupler is used to isolate the Raspberry Pi and the rest
of the system from high voltages and currents, separating the systems for safety. The
connections made to PCA9685 are shown in Figure 6.2.

6.1.2
Sensor wiring
The sensors used in the climate unit are SHT30 temperature and humidity sensors, which
are connected to the Raspberry Pi using I2C communication. The SHT30 sensors use
a 4-pin connection with VCC, GND, SDA, and SCL pins (VCC: power, GND: ground,
SDA: I2C data, SCL: I2C clock) and are programmed from the factory to have a unique
I2C address. It is used to communicate with the specific device, even though multiple
sensors can be connected to the same I2C interface. Since multiple SHT30 sensors were
needed to measure temperature and humidity in different locations within the enclosure, a
multiplexer (specifically TCA9548A) is used to connect multiple sensors to the same I2C
bus. The TCA9548A is an 8-channel I2C multiplexer that allows multiple I2C devices
to be connected to the same bus.
The TCA9548A is controlled by the Raspberry Pi
using I2C communication, and it allows the Raspberry Pi to select which SHT30 sensor
to communicate with by enabling the corresponding channel. The connections made to
TCA9548A are shown in Figure 6.3a.

6.2
Module identification
Two main features are implemented to identify the modules connected to the system. The
first feature is to identify the module placement within the system, and the second feature
is to identify the module type automatically.
6.2.1
Module placement
The pogo pin assemblies have been soldered to a long array of wires running down the back
of the enclosure with male USB 3.0 headers at the other end. 9 pogo pins USB headers
Modular Incubation and Monitoring System for Sample Storage in High-Throughput Screening
63
==PAGE:72==
is soldered for each of the 10 module placements, resulting in 180 solder joints. This was
done by hand, but might be ordered as a custom cable for future assemblies to save time.
The pogo pin wires with USB 3.0 headers are connected to two USB hubs, one for the top
row and one for the bottom row. The USB hubs are connected to a Raspberry Pi, which
is responsible for controlling the modules and reading the modules data. By utilizing the
usb-devices command within the Linux terminal, the USB hubs and individual USB
input headers can be identified, as it provides detailed information about the connected
USB devices. The command lists all USB devices connected to the system, including their
bus number, device number, and other relevant information. This information can be used
to identify the specific USB headers and their corresponding modules.
A USB has different parameters, including bus number, level number, and port number.
The bus number indicates the USB bus to which the device is connected, the level number
indicates the level of the device in the USB hierarchy, and the port number indicates the
port in the USB hub to which the device is connected. The following show the identified
USB headers using the 2 USB hubs:

The placement of every module within the system is determined by Table 6.2, where the
index starts from the left bottom row and counts up from left to right (index 1-5). The
second row is counted in the same way starting from the left top row (index 6-10). One hub
connected to bus 1 controls all modules in the bottom row, while the other hub connected
to bus 3 controls all modules in the top row.
The USB hubs have 7 ports each, but only 5 ports are used for the modules. Normal hubs
have a constant level number, where the index would be equal to the port number minus
one, but the USB hubs used in this project have a level number of 2 and 3, hence the
importance of following Table 6.2 to identify the modules.
Mapping of connected modules and their placement within the system is now achieved.
This is a viable futureproofing feature for when a robotic arm is implemented to place
sample plates on specific plate holders within a certain module.
6.2.2
Module type
In order to identify the module type automatically, a microcontroller should be imple-
mented in the module. All microcontrollers or devices have a unique serial number, which
is automatically gathered using the usb-devices command. If a module is connected
without a microcontroller, the module type can be identified by the user interface in a
drop-down menu. This ensures that the certain device/module type connected with a
unique serial number can be saved in a simple database consisting of the module type
and serial number. So, next time the module is connected, the system will automatically
identify the module type and load the correct settings based on the saved serial number
database.
Some examples of this automatic identification protocol have been made for the dot, stor-
age, and present plate modules. Each module has a ESP32-C3 microcontroller soldered
64
Modular Incubation and Monitoring System for Sample Storage in High-Throughput Screening
==PAGE:73==
to a USB 2.0 connection (pin 1-4 in Figure 5.11a) in the module. The ESP32-C3 mi-
crocontroller is a low-power, low-cost microcontroller that is easily programmed using the
Arduino IDE. The microcontroller is programmed to send its module type when connected
and if the Raspberry Pi sends a request for the module type indicated as ”?”. So when
a module is connected, the Raspberry Pi sends a request for the module type, and the
ESP32-C3 microcontroller responds with its module type. No unique features are added
to the interface, other than the appearance of the visual module and a system count of
the 26 added sample plates within the enclosure for the storage module. The code for the
storage module is shown in Appendix F.2, which is identical to the dot module, with its
module type changed to ”Dot” instead of ”Storage” in the code.
The present plate module has some added communication, as it has to turn the sample
plate 90 degrees with a servo motor when the sample plate is placed in the module. This
is done by adding two extra serial inputs: ”0” and ”1” in addition to ”?”. The ESP32-C3
microcontroller is coded to stay at 0 degrees pointing towards the doors and then turn 90
degrees when ”1” is received from the Raspberry Pi. It is coded to have a constant low
constant acceleration with variable velocity, ensuring that the samples are not shaken or
damaged during the turning process. The code for the present plate module is shown in
Appendix F.3. This added communication only shows on the motion screen if a present
plate module is connected at a given location in the system.
6.3
Software
The software for the modular system is implemented in Python, utilizing Kivy and
KivyMD libraries to create a modern, touch-friendly user interface. In total, around 2000
lines of code have been developed to manage all aspects of the system, including module
identification, climate control, device communication, and user interaction. The scripts
are structured to provide a robust and flexible foundation for current functionality and
future expansion, ensuring that the system remains user-friendly and adaptable as new
modules and features are added. The integration of KivyMD allows for a clean, intuitive
Material Design interface, making it easy for users to monitor and control the system’s
various functions.
A key aspect of maintaining a stable environment within the enclosure is precise temper-
ature and humidity regulation. This is achieved using a Proportional Integral Derivative
(PID) control algorithm, which is widely used in industrial and laboratory automation for
its effectiveness in managing dynamic systems. The PID controller continuously moni-
tors sensor data and adjusts the heating and cooling elements to minimize the difference
between the measured values and the desired setpoints. By automatically compensating
for disturbances and system delays, the PID control ensures that the enclosure maintains
optimal conditions for the intended applications.
6.3.1
PID control
The enclosure has a total of six temperature and humidity sensors (SHT30) distributed
across the top and bottom rows, with three sensors in each row. The sensors are used
to measure temperature and humidity of the enclosure and to control the heating and
cooling of the climate unit. They are connected to the microcontroller (Raspberry Pi),
which is responsible for reading the sensor data and controlling the heating and cooling
of the climate unit according to the data.
The heating and cooling of the climate unit is controlled by a PID controller within the
microcontroller using the library simple-pid. The PID controller is a control loop feed-
back mechanism widely used in industrial control systems. A PID controller continuously
Modular Incubation and Monitoring System for Sample Storage in High-Throughput Screening
65
==PAGE:74==
calculates an error value as the difference between a desired setpoint and a measured
process variable and applies a correction based on proportional, integral and derivative
terms.
Several approaches exist to tune a PID feedback loop.
The most effective techniques
typically involve creating a model and selecting the P, I, and D parameters based on
the dynamic characteristics of the system. Multiple methods exist for tuning PID con-
trollers[28], including:
• Manual tuning: It involves adjusting the P, I, and D parameters by hand until
the desired performance is achieved. This method can be time-consuming, but can
be effective following a systematic approach.
• Ziegler–Nichols method: The method is based on the idea of setting the I and D
gains to zero and increasing the P gain until the output of the loop oscillates. The
ultimate gain and oscillation period are then used to set the PID parameters.
• Tyreus–Luyben method: The method is a variation of the Ziegler–Nichols method
that is more suitable for systems with significant time delay. It uses the ultimate
gain and oscillation period to set the PID parameters, but it also takes into account
the time delay of the system.
• Cohen–Coon method: The method is a more advanced technique that uses the
process reaction curve to determine the PID parameters. This method is particularly
useful for systems with a significant time delay.
No single tuning method is universally applicable and best suited. The popular Ziegler–
Nichols method was initially tried, but it was diﬀicult to estimate the ultimate gain and
oscillation period because of the system’s time delay and changes in ambient temperature.
Time constraints and complexity of the system resulted in the decision to use manual
tuning. The PID parameters were adjusted by hand until the desired performance was
achieved using the following systematic approach.
Parameter Rise time
Overshoot
Settling
time
Steady-state
error
Stability
Kp
Decrease
Increase
Small
change
Decrease
Degrade
Ki
Decrease
Increase
Increase
Eliminate
Degrade
Kd
Minor
change
Decrease
Decrease
No effect (in
theory)
Improve (if
Kd is
small)
Table 6.3: Effects of increasing PID parameters independently[28]
Using an iterative approach, the PID parameters were adjusted one at a time. The propor-
tional gain (Kp) was increased until the system started to make oscillations. The integral
gain (Ki) was then increased to eliminate steady-state error. Finally, the derivative gain
(Kd) was adjusted to improve stability and reduce overshoot.
The PID parameters were set to Kp = 10, Ki = 0.5, and Kd = 0.2. These values were
chosen based on the response of the heaters to heat up the enclosure and provided a
relatively aggressive but good balance between performance and stability.
66
Modular Incubation and Monitoring System for Sample Storage in High-Throughput Screening
==PAGE:75==
One of the main challenges with the PID controller was the time delay in the system. The
Peltier module and PTC heater respond relatively quickly, but the enclosure temperature
response was slow with a significant time delay, making it diﬀicult and time consuming to
tune the PID parameters. The time delay was caused by the large thermal mass of the
enclosure, which results in small variations between different PID values.
To further enhance the PID controller’s performance, a cascade control strategy can be
considered. Cascade control involves using two control loops, each with its own controller,
to achieve more precise and stable regulation. In this context, two temperature sensors
could be used, one positioned near the heater and another further in the enclosure.
The outer primary PID controller monitors the air temperature of the enclosure and deter-
mines the desired heater temperature setpoint based on the difference between the target
and measured air temperatures. This setpoint is then passed to the inner secondary PID
controller, which directly regulates the heater by comparing the heater’s actual temper-
ature (measured by the sensor attached to the heater) to the setpoint provided by the
outer loop.
This arrangement allows each controller to be tuned according to the dynamics of its re-
spective subsystem. The outer loop addresses the slower thermal response of the enclosure
air, while the inner loop manages the faster response of the heater. By separating control
tasks, cascade control can improve overall system stability, reduce overshoot, and achieve
faster settling times compared to a single-loop PID controller.[28]

Software summary
In summary, the software developed for this modular system provides a robust, flexible,
and user-friendly platform to control and monitor all aspects of the machine.
Using
Python, Kivy, and KivyMD, the interface is modern and intuitive, supporting real-time
data visualization, device management, and system operations. The modular architecture
ensures that new features and hardware can be integrated with minimal effort, making
the system adaptable to future requirements and technological advancements. Overall, the
software forms a solid foundation for reliable operation, eﬀicient workflow, and continued
development of the modular incubation HTS platform.
