Modular Incubation and Monitoring System for Sample Storage in High-Throughput Screening
==PAGE:83==
7
Final Product
The final product is a fully functional, modular and climate-controlled incubation system
developed for HTS applications. It is shown in Figure 7.1a and features a robust stainless
steel enclosure with high insulation performance, ensuring stable and uniform temperature
and humidity conditions. The system integrates advanced climate control components, in-
cluding Peltier modules, PTC heaters, and ultrasonic humidifiers, managed by distributed
PID controllers for precise environmental regulation.
(a) Front of final product.
(b) Back of final product.
Figure 7.1: Overview of the final product with a few modules.
A key innovation of this project is the modular framework, which allows users to quickly
interchange functional modules using pogo pin connectors and T-slot fastenings.
This
design supports a wide range of experimental workflows and enables seamless integra-
tion with automation platforms, such as robotic arms. To demonstrate the versatility of
the system, four modules were designed and manufactured for the machine, as shown in
Figure 7.1a. These modules show how the platform can be adapted for different tasks
and experimental requirements. The electronics and software architecture, centered on a
Raspberry Pi 5 and a Python-based GUI, provide intuitive control, real-time monitoring,
and automatic module recognition, further enhancing flexibility and ease of use.
The design of the final product incorporates correct kinematic principles to ensure robust
mechanical performance and precise alignment of all interlocking parts. Attention was
given to the placement and tolerances of components, minimizing unwanted play and en-
suring reliable operation of module exchanges. Furthermore, the system was developed
with DFM and DFA principles in mind. This approach helped simplify the machining
and fabrication processes, reduce the number of unique parts, and streamline the assem-
bly procedures. As a result, the product is not only robust, but also cost-effective and
straightforward to manufacture and assemble.
Overall, the final product represents an engineered solution that balances performance,
adaptability, and manufacturability. This chapter provides details of the final product,
including safety and cleaning considerations, Bill of Materials (BOM), and assembly pro-
cedures.
Modular Incubation and Monitoring System for Sample Storage in High-Throughput Screening
75
==PAGE:84==
7.1
Safety and cleaning considerations
There are many safety and cleaning documents that are required for ISO proposals, such as
ISO 14644 (Cleanrooms and associated controlled environments) and ISO 13485 (Medical
devices, quality management systems). These standards specify requirements for clean-
liness, contamination control, and cleaning and maintenance procedures to ensure a safe
and sterile environment.
For laboratory and medical equipment, compliance with these ISO standards is essential
to guarantee that the product can be used safely in regulated environments and meets
international requirements for hygiene and contamination control.
Adherence to these
standards not only ensures user safety, but also facilitates certification and acceptance in
professional and clinical settings.
In order to fulfill these requirements, a UV light strip was placed along with the white
LED strip light on the top front of the enclosure. This should help the user clean the main
surfaces and most importantly, the recirculated air in the machine. It should not be done
while the samples are incubating, as this might kill the samples, but rather be a start and
stop safety routine to kill unwanted bacteria.
7.1.1
Decontamination
Ultraviolet (UV) light is widely used for microbial decontamination. The effectiveness of
UV sterilization depends on wavelength, intensity, and exposure time. The most effective
wavelength is often 254nm (UV-C). The required UV dose is given by:
UV Dose = UV Intensity · Time
[ mJ
cm2
]
(7.1)
where UV Intensity is in
[ mW
cm2
]
and time is in seconds [s]. The formula shows that having
a higher intensity or a longer exposure time will increase the UV dose, resulting in the
elimination of microorganisms. Table 7.1 presents estimates of the recommended UV doses
required to eliminate various microorganisms.
Target
UV dose
[ mJ
cm2
]
Optimal wavelength [nm]
Bacteria (E. coli, Salmonella)
2–10
254
Viruses (Influenza, SARS-CoV-2)
10–40
254
Fungal Spores (Aspergillus)
50–100
254
Bacterial Spores (Bacillus)
100–200
254
Table 7.1: UV dose estimations and optimal wavelength for decontamination.[29]
The optimal wavelength for decontamination is typically around 254nm. The UV light
strip mounted on the top front of the enclosure is intended to decontaminate air and
surfaces within the enclosure. The installed light strip operates at a wavelength of ap-
proximately 400, nm, which lies in the near-UV (UV-A) region. According to Table 7.1,
the most effective wavelength for microbial inactivation is 254, nm, which falls within the
UV-C range. Therefore, the selected light source is not optimal for sterilization, as UV-A
light is significantly less effective in damaging microorganisms.
However, this strip was chosen based on practical considerations such as cost, availability,
and its high LED density. With 240 LEDs per meter and a total of approximately 360
LEDs in the enclosure, the system is capable of generating relatively high overall irra-
diance. Although the eﬀiciency at 400nm is low, the high LED count can contribute to
76
Modular Incubation and Monitoring System for Sample Storage in High-Throughput Screening
==PAGE:85==
some level of microbial reduction through a prolonged exposure time. These effects will
be experimentally evaluated to determine their practical impact on the enclosure decon-
tamination performance.
7.2
Bill of Materials (BOM)
A little more than 120 distinct items are used in the machine with a total of around 300
parts plus fasteners. A systematic list of all components and materials was required to keep
track of the parts needed and where in the process of ordering, revisions, manufacturing,
and assembling the parts where. This was done to ensure that all parts were accounted for
and to avoid any delays in the assembly process. The Bill of Materials (BOM) is divided
into five categories:
Sheet Plates
Alu Extrusions
Electronics
3D Printed
Other
The BOM is also divided into subcategories in the tables, making it easier to find specific
items. The BOM includes information such as part numbers, descriptions, and quantities
needed for each assembly. It is found in Appendix D.
The BOM was initially created in a spreadsheet format, allowing easy tracking and updat-
ing of the parts process in the material supply chain. The BOM was also used to create a
cost estimate for the project, which was important for budgeting and planning purposes.
The BOM was continuously updated throughout the project to reflect any changes in the
design or materials used. The BOM has now been converted to categorized tables for
easier reading, which is to be found in Appendix D.
Some items did not end up being used or were used for development purposes only. They
have not been included in the BOM tables. The BOM tables is a complete list of all
parts used in the enclosure, with a comprehensive overview of the main components and
materials used in the project.
The module BOM amount is ordered to present all module types and not fill out the
enclosure. More shells should be ordered from the toolshop in 427 in order to fill out
the enclosure in addition to its 3D printed parts. The stainless steel sheet materials are
ordered to the toolshop and ready to be cut.
The item number provided in Table D.3 is the item number of the oﬀicial Bosch Rexroth
supplier. Similar aluminum extrusion systems can be used for a lower price. In this project
www.myaluprofil.de was used with its I-Type profiles, which is a much cheaper alternative
to Bosch Rexroth. The profiles are a bit different as it is I-type instead of B-type, which
Bosch uses. The I-type profiles have a bit more third-party accessories available, while
having a slightly smaller groove of 8mm for 40x40 and 30x30 profiles instead of the 10mm
groove that Bosch uses for their 40x40.
This results in M6 being the best and most
common assembly option for both the 40x40 and 30x30 profiles.
7.3
Assembly procedure
Based of the BOM, an assembly procedure was created to ensure that all parts were
assembled in the correct order if the machine were to be replicated, repaired, or modified.
The assembly procedure is divided into three main sections:
1. Enclosure Assembly
2. Electronics Assembly
3. Module Assembly
The enclosure assembly includes the assembly of all extrusions with the outer and inner
panels, the base and top panels, and the electronic panel. Electronics includes the assembly
Modular Incubation and Monitoring System for Sample Storage in High-Throughput Screening
77
==PAGE:86==
of electronic components, such as the Raspberry Pi, controllers, and other components. For
wiring the electronics, a wiring diagram within the electronics section should be followed
in section 6. The module assembly includes the assembly of the modules and may depend
on the specific module being assembled.
7.3.1
Enclosure assembly
The enclosure assembly is the first step in the assembly procedure. It also consists of some
electronic wiring, as it involves dependent assembly steps. The assembly procedure is as
follows:
1. Assemble corner extrusions: Assemble corner extrusions (40x40LRx1256mm)
with the top extrusions (40x40LRx1354mm and 40x40LRx420mm) using I-type con-
nection set R40-90 °.
2. Mount outer back and side plates: Mount the outer back plate and outer side
plates using M6x12mm screws and T-nuts. Attach sealing foam to the flanges of the
plates to ensure a good seal.
3. Mount remaining extrusions: Mount the rest of the extrusions up against the
plates using the internal angle slot 8 and angle sets (40x40).
4. Mount base plate: Mount the base plate on top of the bottom extrusions using
M6x12mm screws and T-nuts.
5. Add insulation to side and back panels: Add insulation to the side panels
and the back panel. Remember to cut out holes for the present plate hole and the
airflow channel in the side walls. Ensure to use aluminum tape to seal the insulation,
leaving no holes or gaps. Make sure to seal the overlapping plates and extrusions
with aluminum tape to ensure no air leaks.
6. Mount top panel and insulation: Place the enclosure on its back and cut out
insulation to fit the top panel. Place the top plate on top of the insulation and
mount it using M6x12mm screws and T-nuts. Ensure that the insulation is sealed
with aluminum tape to ensure no air leaks. Mount T-tracks on the top panel or at
least the screws and nuts, as they can’t be mounted after the top panel is mounted.
7. Prepare inner side panels: Prepare the inner side panels separately by attaching
the fan blowers with long countersunk M3 screws and nuts. Ensure the use of a
gasket/double-sided sealing tape between the fan and the panel to ensure no air
leaks. Add the airflow channel using double sided tape and by snapping the end into
the cutout on the bottom of the side panel.
8. Solder pogo pin cables: Separately solder all 10x pogo pins with a long 9 wire
cable of around 1.5m length. Do the same in the other end of the cable, but with
male USB connectors. Use a heat shrink tube to cover all soldering joints and ensure
good connection. Test the connection by plugging the cable into a computer and
checking if data can be read and written to a connected device.
9. Mount pogo pin assemblies: Mount all 10x pogo pin assemblies using a press fit
in the inner back plate. Make them all run straight down to the bottom of the plate.
10. Install inner back plate: Place the inner back plate with the pogo cables into
the enclosure laying on its back. Ensure that the cables are not pinched or damaged
during the process and that they are long enough to come together at the bottom
of the enclosure.
78
Modular Incubation and Monitoring System for Sample Storage in High-Throughput Screening
==PAGE:87==
11. Solder temperature sensors: Solder the six temperature sensors with a long 4
wire cable of around 2.5m length. Use a heat shrink tube to cover all soldering joints
and ensure a good connection. Test the connection by plugging the cable into the
Raspberry Pi and checking if data can be read from the sensors. Use a cable sleeve
of 30cm at the sensor end to cover the colored cables and make it look nicer.
12. Solder LED and UV light strip: Solder the LED and UV light strip with long
power cables of around 2m length.
13. Wire fans and sensors: Solder longer cables to the fan blowers, around 2m length
and mount the SHT30 temperature sensors with a long cable on each of the side
plates. Also mount the two light strip cables along the air channel. Write the name
of each cable on the ends and route them through the cable hole in the side plates.
14. Mount inner side panels: Foam adhesive tape should be added to the flanges of
the side panels before sliding the plates into the cutouts on the back plate. Fasten
it with the countersunk M4 screws with T-nuts at the outer edge of the side plates.
Ensure that there is enough space made in the insulation for the air channel and
cables to fit.
15. Mount module base: Mount the module base at the bottom inside of the enclosure
and slide it slowly into the gap between the extrusion and inner back plate, without
pinching any cables. Ensure that the cables fall into the cable holes of the module
base when sliding it in. The module base should be mounted to the base plate using
M6 screws and nuts.
16. Assemble doors: The doors can be made separately using the 30x30 extrusions
mounted together with the internal angle slot 8. Caps are added to the ends of the
extrusions to ensure a plane face to seal against the enclosure.
17. Mount inner polycarbonate panels: The inner polycarbonate panels are mounted
using M4 countersunk screws and T-nuts, on top of groove sealing used in the ex-
trusions.
18. Mount outer polycarbonate panels: Rubber edge sealing is added to the edges
of the outer polycarbonate panels. The outer polycarbonate panels are mounted
using four M4 countersunk screws threaded into 3D printed corner brackets. The
corner brackets snap into the groove of the extrusions.
Extra thin brackets are
snapped into the groove along the edges of the panels to ensure they stay flush with
the extrusion.
19. Mount hinges: Hinges are manufactured and mounted to the doors and the enclo-
sure using one M6 on each hinge. The hinges have a groove align feature to ensure
that the doors are aligned with the enclosure, which is why only one screw is needed
for each hinge. The aligning feature is removed on the hinges mounted on the 30x30
door extrusions, as the groove is not deep enough for the T-nuts to fit.
20. Mount door handles: 3D printed door handles are mounted using M4 screws
threaded into the center of the extrusion.
21. Install doors: The doors are mounted to the enclosure using the detachable hinges
and 5mm sealing foam is added around the edges of the doors to ensure a good seal.
22. Mount screen: The screen with the 3D printed mount is mounted to the front
using M4 screws. Cabling is routed through cable holes under the base plate.
Modular Incubation and Monitoring System for Sample Storage in High-Throughput Screening
79
==PAGE:88==
23. Mount top SHT30 sensors: The bottom three SHT30 sensors are mounted on
the top and routed up through the bottom of the enclosure.
24. Mount front insulation and plate: Insulation is added to the front panel and
the inner front plate is mounted into cutouts in the base plate. It is fastened to the
front extrusion using countersunk M4 screws and T-nuts.
25. Mount magnetic bracket: A 3D printed magnetic bracket is mounted to the front
extrusion to hold the magnets that keep the front doors closed.
26. Install magnets: Rubber coated magnets are screwed into the 3D printed magnetic
bracket and the inner top plate which has threaded holes for the magnets to screw
into.
27. Mount bottom SHT30 sensors: The bottom three SHT30 temperature sensors
are mounted onto the inner front plate and the magnetic bracket using double sided
tape.
28. Mount light strip tray: A light strip tray is mounted to the top front of the top
plate using double sided tape. The light and UV strip is mounted into the tray and
a see-through cover is added to the tray to protect the strips.
7.3.2
Electronics assembly
The electronics assembly is the second step in the assembly procedure. The assembly
procedure is as follows:
1. Mount cooler and breakout board: A cooler with a fan is mounted with thermal
paste to the Raspberry Pi 5. On top, an SSD kit is mounted and connected. Lastly,
a breakout board is mounted to expand the GPIO pins to socket headers, making it
easier to connect wires to.
2. Install Raspberry Pi assembly: The Raspberry Pi 5 assembly is mounted to a
3D printed cover using M2.5x6mm screws. It should allow easy access to the GPIO
pins and USB ports.
3. Connect display: A Raspberry Pi Touch Display 2 is connected using an extended
FPC-22 to FPC-15 cable, which transfers the screen and touch signals to the Rasp-
berry Pi.
4. Mount I2C multiplexer: An I2C Multiplexer TCA9548A is mounted to the back
of the electronic housing. It is connected using I2C and power cables. This allows
for multiple identical I2C devices to be connected, like the six SHT30 temperature
sensors, which are connected to channel 0-5.
5. Mount PWM controller: A PWM channel I2C controller PCA9685PW is also
mounted to the back of the electronic housing. It is connected using I2C and power
cables. This allows for multiple PWM devices to be controlled as the Raspberry Pi
only has a few PWM ports.
6. Connect actuators: Fans, heaters, coolers, and lights are connected to the PCA9685PW
using the PWM channels, which can be seen in the wiring diagram in section 6.
7. Install power supplies: A 5V, 12V, and 24V power supply is mounted in the
center of the electronic housing. The 5V power supply powers the Raspberry Pi,
display, and peripherals. The 12V power supply powers the fans and coolers, while
the 24V power supply powers the lights, heaters, and heater fans.
80
Modular Incubation and Monitoring System for Sample Storage in High-Throughput Screening
==PAGE:89==
8. Connect MOSFET regulators: Every device is connected to a PWM MOSFET
regulator (LR7843) that controls the power to the device. The MOSFET regulators
are connected to the PCA9685PW using PWM channels and to the power supply
using the appropriate voltage. This ensures that the devices can be controlled from
0-100% power, allowing for precise control of the devices.
9. Install USB hubs and connect pogo pins: Two USB hubs are mounted to the
back center of the electronic housing and connected to the Raspberry Pi using its
USB 3.0 ports. Every pogo pin wire end is connected to the USB hubs, with the top
row connected to the top USB hub and the bottom row connected to the bottom
USB hub. This follows the identification of every module placement.
10. Assemble and mount climate units: The climate units consist of a cooler kit,
a heater kit, and an ultrasonic humidifier kit.
The cooler kit is made of a 12V
Peltier module (TEC1-12706) in an aluminum fin sandwich and two 12V fans. The
heater kit is made of a 24V PTC heater (24V 200W) and a 24V fan. The ultrasonic
humidifier kit is made of a 5V ultrasonic humidifier. This is all mounted together
in the 3D printed climate unit housing, which is mounted to the bottom base plate
using two M4 screws and nuts. The climate unit is connected to the PCA9685PW
using PWM channels and to the power supply using the appropriate voltage.
7.3.3
Module assembly
The module assembly is the third step in the assembly procedure. This is more specific
to the individual module, but the main assembly procedure is as follows:
1. Mount 3D printed top: The 3D printed top is mounted to the shell using four
countersunk M4 screws.
2. Install pogo pins: The pogo pins are pressed into the pogo pin mount and placed
between the bottom and shell part.
3. Mount bottom part: The bottom part is mounted to the shell using four coun-
tersunk M4 screws.
4. Install track: A track is mounted to the bottom part using two countersunk M4
screws. The track is used to guide the module into the enclosure and to ensure that
it is aligned with the pogo pins.
5. Solder to pogo pins and mount controller: Soldering to pogo pins can be done
with an ESP32-C3 device or another USB device.
The ESP32-C3 is the recom-
mended development board as it has all the necessary features, is easy to program,
and is low in price. The ESP32-C3 is mounted to the bottom electronic housing
using double adhesive tape.
6. Close bottom part: The bottom part is closed with two 3D printed snap covers.
7. Mount backplate (if applicable): If a module is made with a backplate, such
as the dot module, the backplate is mounted to the shell using four countersunk
M4 screws. In between the shell and the backplate, a 3D printed spacer and thread
mount is placed to ensure that the backplate is aligned.
Modular Incubation and Monitoring System for Sample Storage in High-Throughput Screening
81
==PAGE:90==
82
Modular Incubation and Monitoring System for Sample Storage in High-Throughput Screening
==PAGE:91==
8
Test and Evaluation
In life science applications, rigorous testing of industrial products is important to evaluate
and ensure that they meet requirements and specifications. This chapter presents the
systematic testing and evaluation of the enclosure system. Key performance aspects such
as temperature control and uniformity, humidity regulation, airflow, and contamination
are assessed through a series of experiments. The results are compared with the initial
design criteria to validate the system’s effectiveness and identify areas for improvement.
Before initializing data logging, a series of troubleshooting tests were conducted to sys-
tematically evaluate the performance of the enclosure in terms of heating, cooling, humid-
ification, airflow, lighting and modularity. These preliminary tests included multiple runs
to fine-tune the initial PID settings and to identify and resolve issues related to software,
wiring, and hardware. This iterative process ensured that the system was functioning
reliably before starting the main experimental tests.
The following sensor readings and tests are performed using the indexing shown in Figure
8.1a. A top and bottom row divided by the section plate divides the two rows, and the
climate system reference is additionally divided into left, middle and right data points.
Three SHT30 temperature and humidity sensors were placed in the bottom row and three
in the top row of the enclosure as seen in Figure 8.1a. They represent the temperature
and humidity at a specific point in the enclosure.
Six SHT30 sensors were chosen to
ensure reliability and uniformity within the enclosure and they were chosen for their high
accuracy of ±2%RH within 0-90%RH and ±0.2°C within 0-65°C.
(a) Section view climate components.
(b) SHT30 temperature and
humidity sensor.
Figure 8.1: Placement and view of SHT30 sensors in the enclosure.

83
==PAGE:92==
8.1
Humidifier test
The ultrasonic humidifier was tested to see how much humidity it could provide in the
enclosure. Concern arose when implementing the electronics that the humidifiers were
close to the power supplies. So, if a leakage from the humidifiers small water tank should
occur, it could lead to a short circuit in the power supplies. Therefore, only the right
humidifier was tested, as it is the farthest away from the power supplies. The humidifier
was tested with the fan on 100% power and the ultrasonic humidifier fully on at 100%.
The humidifier was on for 10min, and the humidity was logged from the SHT30 sensors
in the enclosure. The results at room temperature (21.5°C) can be seen in Figure 8.2.

Row 2 - Left Humidity
Row 2 - Middle Humidity
Row 2 - Right Humidity
Figure 8.2: Humidity test with ultrasonic humidifier.
Figure 8.2 shows that the humidity in the lower right corner of the enclosure rises from
around 60% to 90% in a period of 10 minutes after the initial start at the 2 minute mark.
The other sensors in the enclosure shows an increase from 55-60% to 65-70% in the same
period of time. On the bottom row 1, the middle sensor has the second highest humidity
because it is closest to the right sensor, while the left sensor has the lowest humidity.
These would of course increase and be more even if the left and middle humidifiers were
turned on, and they might be able to provide an even higher maximum humidity in the
enclosure.
As the relative humidity depends on the temperature in the enclosure, the increase seen in
Figure 8.2 is not the same as at 30°C and 37°C. The relative humidity follows the Clausius-
Clapeyron relation, meaning that as the temperature increases, the air’s capacity to hold
water vapor rises exponentially.
Therefore, the same absolute amount of water vapor
results in a lower relative humidity at higher temperatures. So, the expected performance
of humidifiers at higher temperatures is that they will take longer time and may not be
able to reach the same relative humidity. Further tests need to validate this assumption.
This short test shows that the ultrasonic humidifier is able to provide a significant amount
of humidity in the enclosure and that it can be used to maintain a high humidity level in
the enclosure. The humidity MVP criteria (Table 1.2) is partially met, as the humidity
can be adjusted and reach a humidity of 90% in the corner of the enclosure at room
temperature using one ultrasonic humidifier. It might also be able to reach higher and a
more even humidity distribution using all three implemented humidifiers, but this was not
tested due to safety concerns and time limitations.

8.2
Cooling test
A cooling test was performed to assess the cooling time and minimum temperature of
the enclosure. The Peltier devices and its fan systems were turned on full power until
conclusions could be drawn from the data points.
The test was initiated from room
temperature at around 21.5°C as seen from the initial values in Figure 8.3.

Figure 8.3: Temperature cooling test
No significant cooling was observed, and the temperature increased slightly from the initial
21.5°C. The rise in temperature might be the result of the power supplies that generate
a lot of heat when running at full power. A slight cooling was observed for the middle
temperatures in the beginning of the test, which could be the power supplies providing a
suﬀicient amount of power for the middle Peltier device over a short period of time.
The Peltier devices and fans run on 12V, and the power supply is rated for 17A. Connecting
a multimeter to the 12V power supply showed that the power supply was not able to
provide all 17A, as it was only able to provide 12.5A. This could be a reason for the
cooling not being as effective as expected, as the Peltier devices (TEC1-12706) are rated
for 6A, resulting in a total of 18A for the three devices plus fans. The fans only draw up
to 0.5A and were therefore not affected by the current limit of the power supply.
To try and improve the cooling, an additional power supply was found, which was rated at
4A at 12V. Adding this in parallel resulted in a total of 21A, which should be suﬀicient for
the three Peltier devices and fans. Still, the test showed no significant cooling and similar
performance, where the current eventually dropped from the power supply. This test with
extra power showed that the power required is only available for a short period of time
and the power supply did not perform as stated in the documentation. In the future, a
new more reliable power supply should be used to ensure that the Peltier devices can run
at full power.
8.3
Heating test
Multiple heating tests were performed to iteratively adjust the PID settings and to evaluate
the heating performance of the enclosure.
The following tests were conducted on the
heaters:
• Heating with maximum airflow and different recirculation levels.
Modular Incubation and Monitoring System for Sample Storage in High-Throughput Screening
85
==PAGE:94==
• Heating with all temperature sensors mean as PID input.
• Heating with a cascade PID controller.
• Heating with temperature sensors in the top row as PID input.
All tests were performed to reach a target temperature of 30 or 37°C, as they are the
most common sample growth temperatures. The initial room temperature was around
21.5°C, but fluctuates considerably depending on the weather and sun conditions, as the
tests were performed in a room with a lot of sunlight, which made it diﬀicult to keep
the temperature stable. The tests were performed with the enclosure closed, doors and
electronic back plate closed.
8.3.1
First logged heat test
A heating test was performed with PID setting set to 10, 0.1, 0.05 for the P, I and D
values, respectively. Each heater has its own PID controller with these settings with input
of the average temperature from the same column of temperature sensors. Meaning, the
left top sensor and the left bottom sensor are averaged and used as input for the left heater
PID controller. The same is done for the middle and right heaters, using the middle and
right temperature sensors respectively. This was made to ensure an even temperature
throughout the enclosure, and its logged temperature results for the six sensors in the
enclosure can be seen in Figure 8.4.
Figure 8.4: Temperature heating test to 37°C.
The initial heat test showed that the temperature sensors in the top row had converged
to a temperature above the target temperature of 37°C, while the bottom row sensors
had a temperature below the target temperature. This makes sense as the heaters target
the average of the top and bottom sensors, resulting in it not having a feedback response
loop based on the temperature difference. The average of all temperature sensors is also
plotted in Figure 8.4, which shows a temperature closely converged to the target tem-
perature of 37°C, but with slight periodic oscillations. This is due to the PID controller
not being properly tuned, which results in the temperature oscillating around the target
temperature.
Sensor temperatures start from around 30°C, resulting in a quick response to the 37°C
target temperature. The middle bottom temperature sensor rises first and has the largest
86
Modular Incubation and Monitoring System for Sample Storage in High-Throughput Screening
==PAGE:95==
oscillations, which indicates that the middle heater is influenced by the two other heaters
and therefore needs to adjust accordingly. The left and right bottom sensors are more
stable and similar to each other, as they might act identically. The left top row temperature
sensor increases more than the other two top row sensors, which indicates that the left
heater is more powerful than the other two heaters. This is expected as the left heater is
close to the power supplies heat exhaust, which helps the left heater to heat up faster.
The overall trends of the top row and bottom row temperature sensors are that they slowly
converge and would probably reach the target temperature if the test were continued for a
longer period of time. The bottom row sensors show a lot of oscillations, which indicates
that the temperature sensors are closely placed to the heaters, making them more sensitive
to the heaters performance. The top row sensors are more stable and show a more even
temperature distribution, which is expected as they are further away from the heaters.
8.3.2
Refinement tests
Several iterative tests were performed to adjust the PID, heater fan and recirculation fan
settings. Trying the recirculation fan settings at 100%, 50%, 20% and 0%, it was found that
the recirculation fan had a significant impact on the difference in air temperature within
the enclosure. It showed that the recirculation fans at 100% divided the air temperature
into columns in the top row. Leaving the left and right temperature similar, but lower than
the middle temperature. This might be the high-pressure zones on the blower fans pulling
air hot air in at the sides, making air in the middle warmer. So, a natural convection with
hot air slowly rising from the heaters and expelling the cool air from the top. Therefore,
a low recirculation fan setting of less than 50% should be used if a fast heating response
time is desired, while the recirculation fan setting can be increased after temperature
convergence.
The heater fans were also tested with different settings, but it was found that the heater
fans should be set to 100% to ensure a fast heating response time. The heater fans are
used to blow the hot air from the heaters into the enclosure, and if they are set to a lower
setting, the hot air will not be distributed evenly in the enclosure. This results in a slower
heating response time and a larger temperature difference between the top and bottom
row temperature sensors.
Cascade PID controllers were also tested, with an inner and an outer PID controller. The
inner PID controller was given the temperature of the lower row temperature sensors,
and the outer PID controller was given the upper row temperature sensors.
Different
PID settings were tested, but the results showed that the cascade PID controller did not
perform better than the initial PID controller. The cascade PID controller was therefore
not used in the final tests, as it did not improve the temperature precision, uniformity or
stability. It might be a good idea to use a cascade PID controller in the future, but it
requires more time, tuning, and testing to get it to work properly.
Several PID settings were tested, but always resulted in a precise mean temperature, but
with a large temperature difference between the top and bottom row temperature sensors.
Therefore, the PID input was changed to react using the top row temperature sensors as
input instead of the average of both row sensors. This was done based on the thought
that the bottom row temperature sensors are too close to the heaters and therefore not
representative of the overall temperature in the enclosure.
The top row temperature
sensors are placed further away from the heaters, and therefore are more representative of
the overall temperature in the enclosure. The following heater tests were performed with
the PID input of the top row temperature sensors.
Modular Incubation and Monitoring System for Sample Storage in High-Throughput Screening
87
==PAGE:96==

with the input from the top row temperature sensors. The heaters were set to heat the
enclosure to 30°C and then hold the temperature stable until the test was stopped at
around 3 hours of logging. The result is shown in Figure 8.5.

The temperature in the enclosure is stable and uniform, with a maximum deviation of

specified with an accuracy of ±0.2 °C [30], meaning each sensor’s reading may deviate by

could be slightly higher or lower than the measured values. This effect is illustrated in

==PAGE:98==
Additional sensor plots were made to illustrate a theoretical maximum temperature, how
heating affects humidity in the enclosure, and how the PWM controller acts and influences
the temperature, which can be seen in Appendix C.
To estimate the maximum temperature which the system is able to, the data from the
37°C test were analyzed using regression and extrapolation of the top row sensors to
predict the maximum temperature that can be achieved with the current setup. This was
done instead of a real enclosure test to avoid power supply overheating and potentially
melting the PLA parts of the climate unit.
A maximum temperature of 42.42°C was
estimated from the regression plot shown in Appendix C.1 and extrapolation using an
exponential decay inverse, as the heater slowly converges to a value, assuming that they
follow an asymptotic curve fit. The resulting first-order thermal system model is given by
the equation:
T(t) = T∞+ (T0 −T∞) · e−t
τ = 42.42 + (21.31 −42.42) · e−
t
41.29
(8.1)
where T(t) is the temperature at time t, T0 is the initial temperature, T∞is the maximum
temperature and τ is the time constant. The parameters were estimated using a least
squares fit to the data points of the 37°C test. Only the fully activated heater time from 5
to 48 min was used in the regression model to obtain an accurate estimate. This formula
converges to the systems theoretical maximum temperature just above 42°C fulfilling the
MVP criteria, but it might differ from the actual maximum temperature that can be
achieved in the incubator.
The humidity plots in Appendix C.2 show that the relative humidity also oscillates as a
result of the temperatures in the enclosure. This oscillation slowly converges, giving a
relative humidity of 30-35% at 30°C and around 20% at 37°C. This is as expected, as
higher temperatures can hold more water vapor, resulting in lower relative humidity. The
humidifiers are therefore a viable solution to ensure that the samples do not dry out in
the enclosure over longer incubation periods.
The PWM plots from Appendix C.3 show the correlation between the PID PWM output
and the temperature in the enclosure, where small changes in the PWM output results in
large temperature oscillations in the bottom row temperatures, propagating to the top row
sensors. These signs show that the PID controller could be further tuned to reduce the
oscillations and improve the temperature stability in the enclosure. The PID controllers
for each sensor also act very differently in the 30°C as they each have their own PID
controller. It could be investigated if the PID controllers perform more uniformly if the
PID inputs slightly influence each other.
90
Modular Incubation and Monitoring System for Sample Storage in High-Throughput Screening
==PAGE:99==
8.4
Temperature uniformity
Temperature precision and uniformity was evaluated from the temperature sensors placed
in the enclosure and a separate external infrared camera. This test gives insight into enclo-
sure temperature uniformity and how well the sensors are able to measure the temperature
in the enclosure. The test was performed with doors and electronic back plate closed.
A setup with an Optris Pi 640i infrared camera was used to measure the temperature in the
enclosure. The camera was placed in front of the enclosure on a table as seen in Figure 8.8.
The camera is calibrated from the factory and comes with software and initial calibration
settings. The camera was set to measure the temperature in the enclosure with a field of
view of its max 640x480 pixels. The camera was set to measure with a temperature range
of −20 to 100 °C leading to the best accuracy according to the documentation.[31]
Optris promises a temperature precision of ±2 °C or ±2%, whichever is greater[31]. Mean-
ing, at the 30 °C temperature test, the camera should be able to measure the temperature
with an accuracy of ±2 °C. The camera should primarily be used to indicate temperature
gradients within the enclosure rather than provide precise temperature measurements, as
the installed SHT30 temperature sensors offer a higher accuracy of ±0.2 °C.
(a) Closed enclosure view
(b) Open enclosure view
Figure 8.8: Experimental setup for temperature measurement infrared camera
Figure 8.8a shows the initial camera setup with the enclosure closed. The enclosure heaters
were then initialized to heat the enclosure up to 30 °C. After around 30min the enclosure
temperature had converged and stabilized at 30 °C. The enclosure was then opened, and
the infrared camera recorded the temperature when the enclosure was open, as seen in
Figure 8.8b. The recording was done within the software provided with the camera, so
the data could be analyzed later on.
The infrared camera and its measurements on the stainless steel backplate within the
enclosure turned out to be a mirror of the temperatures around the room. Its infrared
wavelengths got mirrored because of the low emissivity of steel at around ε ≈0.1, resulting
in camera, computer, and body heat disturbing the measurements. Cardboard panels were
used instead, as it has a high emissivity of ε ≈0.9. They were placed in the two rows
of the enclosure and indicate the temperature gradient of the back wall. This is seen in
Figure 8.8b and made it possible to measure the temperature in the enclosure without
disturbances from the steel backplate.
The results of this test are seen on a static image in Figure 8.9. The camera has a fish
eye lens, which induced a curved distortion in the image. The image was corrected with
Modular Incubation and Monitoring System for Sample Storage in High-Throughput Screening

==PAGE:100==
software provided with the camera, but the distortion is still visible in the image.

of the temperature spots: Temp (row, column). Scale applies for all four images.

and omits the temperatures below and above this range to ensure that the temperature

Heat test 2 has the highest temperature deviation and measures up to 31.40°C in the

through the left side panel up into the enclosure. It could also be a camera error, as the
side panel is steel and hard to read, or a misalignment of the cardboard panel. The error
is assumed to be small and might not affect the samples placed in the enclosure, as their
temperature is equal to the air temperature.
Figure 8.9 shows slight signs of flow separation on the section plate in the middle of the
enclosure. The color is a bit more red above the section plate, while being more uniform
at the bottom row. This is reflected in the temperature readings presented in Table 8.2.
The overall results show that the temperature is quite evenly distributed within the en-
closure, with a maximum deviation of 1.4°C between the highest and lowest temperature
in the same test. The overall mean temperature is around 30.16°C with a standard de-
viation of 0.50°C, indicating that the temperature is quite stable and uniform within the
enclosure. From the table, 22 of the 24 readings are within the MVP criteria of ±1°C as
seen in Table 1.2 at the 30°C test. Based on these four tests, the enclosure can maintain
a stable temperature uniformity within ±1°C at 30°C, 92.3% of the time.
Modular Incubation and Monitoring System for Sample Storage in High-Throughput Screening
93
==PAGE:102==
Note that the infrared camera measures the temperature of the cardboard panels at the
back of the enclosure, which are assumed to be at the same temperature as the surrounding
air. Thus, these readings primarily indicate temperature gradients within the enclosure
rather than the precise air temperature. For accurate temperature measurements, the
installed SHT30 sensors with a higher accuracy of ±0.2 °C should be relied upon, while
the camera (accuracy of ±2 °C at 30 °C) is best used to visualize temperature uniformity.
8.5
Airflow
To evaluate the airflow in the enclosure for comparison with the simulation results, a
humidity and smoke test was performed. The airflow could not be evaluated with the
ultrasonic humidifiers, as they did not produce enough humidity that could be visualized.
They only produce a small amount, which is diﬀicult to visualize with the relatively low
airflow in the enclosure.
Figure 8.10: Smoke test with airflow
indication pattern on right half of
the enclosure.
Instead, a smoke test was performed to visualize air-
flow in the enclosure. The smoke test was performed
with a smoke machine using FSM5H smoke fluid,
which is a water based fluid that produces white
smoke when heated. The smoke is made of water
and glycerin, which is a common ingredient in smoke
machines. The FSM5H smoke fluid is non-toxic and
biodegradable, making it safe to use.
The smoke is generated by heating the fluid and di-
rected towards the right climate unit with a fan.
The climate unit blows the smoke into the enclo-
sure, allowing the airflow patterns to be visualized
and recorded with a camera. Images of the airflow
patterns in the enclosure can be seen in Figure 8.10.
The airflow was quite hard to visualize in a still

age to indicate the airflow direction. The arrows are
based on the smoke patterns seen from the record-
ing. The smoke was directed at the right climate
unit, but from the recording it was seen that some
also entered the middle climate unit. The airflow
visualized on Figure 8.10 is assumed to be mirrored on the left half of the enclosure.
The airflow patterns show that the airflow is directed from the bottom of the enclosure
and up towards the top up against the side wall and the center in the enclosure. Reduced
smoke above the right heater confirms effective air distribution by the diffuser. The limited
vertical smoke flow suggests the diffuser side angles may be too angled, restricting upward
air direction from the climate unit.
8.6
Contamination evaluation
A contamination test was performed to evaluate the contamination level of the enclosure
and whether 400nm UV light and/or air flow makes a difference. Ager petri dishes were
used to grow bacteria and fungi from the air, as agar provides a nutrient rich environment
for microorganisms to grow. Agar is a gelatinous substance derived from red algae and is
used as a solid growth medium in petri dishes for culturing microorganisms. Four tests
with triplets of agar petri dishes were carried out:
94
Modular Incubation and Monitoring System for Sample Storage in High-Throughput Screening
==PAGE:103==
• Reference sample: Ambient room air
• Sample 1: In enclosure with no airflow and no UV light
• Sample 2: In enclosure with UV light
• Sample 3: In enclosure with airflow and UV light
The tests were performed with the doors and electronic backplate closed. It was performed
over a period of one hour each, where the petri dishes were placed in the enclosure or
ambient room with lid open. Then they were closed and placed away with lid on for a
week to ensure growth, if any.
The results can be seen in Figure 8.11 and show that the reference sample and enclosure
had a lot of growth, while the enclosure with UV and airflow had little to no growth.
Reference
Enclosure
UV
Airflow + UV
Figure 8.11: Each column shows the three replicate petri dishes for each condition.
Figure 8.11 shows the results of the contamination test, where each column represents a
different condition. The first column is the reference samples from ambient room air, the
second column is the samples taken from the enclosure with no airflow and no UV light,
the third column is the samples taken from the enclosure with UV light, and the fourth
column is the samples taken from the enclosure with airflow and UV light.
The reference sample shows a lot of growth, which is expected, as it is taken from ambient
room air. The second column from the enclosure shows slightly less organisms and growth
compared to the reference samples, which indicates that the enclosure is not completely
sterile and has some contamination even with closed doors and electronic backplate. The
third column with UV light shows a significant reduction in growth with only a few colonies
Modular Incubation and Monitoring System for Sample Storage in High-Throughput Screening
95
==PAGE:104==
present, indicating that UV light is effective in reducing contamination. The fourth column
with airflow and UV light shows the least amount of growth, with only one significant
colony present. This indicates that the combination of airflow and UV light is effective in
reducing contamination.
The reduction of contamination with UV light was not expected, as UV light has to
penetrate its own mounting cover.
All while the UV light only was rated for around
400nm, which is not the most effective wavelength for eliminating bacteria. It might be
that the UV light strip has a wide range of wavelengths which it produces, going below
400nm and therefore being effective in eliminating bacteria.
The even lower contamination with airflow and UV light could be due to the airflow
effectively removing the air from the bottom and up against the UV light strip where it
kills the bacteria before recirculating the air back down in the side walls. The upward
facing airflow ensures that all contamination moves up and is exposed to UV light, while
not falling into the samples.
These results indicate that the enclosure is effective in reducing contamination with the
use of UV light and airflow. The enclosure can therefore be considered to have a low con-
tamination level, which is important for maintaining a sterile environment for experiments
and samples. It also allows for future modules and feature expansion, where samples can
be placed without lid on. This makes the machine able to perform pipetting, plating, and
other developing experiments without contamination of samples.

8.7
Summary and evaluation of criteria
This section summarizes the extensive testing performed throughout this chapter and
evaluates the system’s performance against the initial MVP criteria.
8.7.1
Validation of temperature estimation methods
To evaluate the temperature accuracy and reliability in the incubation system, a box plot
was conducted with the three different temperature estimation approaches: CFD sim-
ulation, temperature sensor readings, and infrared camera measurements. This provides
insight into both the simulated performance limits and practical measurement capabilities.
Temperature data was collected from all three measurement methods at the target setpoint
of 30°C to enable direct comparison of precision, accuracy, and uniformity characteristics.
The CFD simulation data represent the numerically ideal performance under perfect con-
ditions, while the SHT30 temperature sensors provide precise point measurements, and
the infrared camera offers temperature mapping for uniformity verification. Figure 8.12
presents a box plot comparison of these methods.
The plot shows the median (solid line), mean (dashed line), quartiles (box), and range
(max/min). Gray shaded regions indicate manufacturer accuracy specifications.
Simulation
SHT30
(±0.2°C spec.)
IR Camera
(±2.0°C spec.)
29.0
29.5
30.0

Temperature [°C]

Manufacturer accuracy
Figure 8.12: Box plot comparison of temperature distributions from simulation, SHT30
sensors, and IR camera measurements during the 30°C heating test.
The CFD simulation data shown in Appendix A.1.3 demonstrate the highest precision and
uniformity with a standard deviation of 0.07°C and a mean temperature of 29.97°C. This
represents the numerical ideal performance under perfect boundary conditions. The simu-
lation results show minimal temperature variation across the computational domain, with
temperatures ranging from 29.76°C to 30.06°C, indicating excellent numerical temperature
uniformity throughout the enclosure volume.
The distribution of simulation probe points validates the effectiveness of the thermal design
and confirms that the heating system configuration is capable of achieving a uniform
temperature distribution under ideal conditions. This provides confidence that observed
variations in experimental measurements are primarily due to real world factors such
as sensor tolerances, measurement uncertainties, and minor environmental disturbances
rather than fundamental design limitations.
Modular Incubation and Monitoring System for Sample Storage in High-Throughput Screening
97
==PAGE:106==
The SHT30 sensor measurements demonstrate good real-world performance with a mean
temperature of 30.01°C and a combined standard deviation of 0.10°C. The three sensors
positioned at left, middle, and right locations in the top row showed individual standard
deviations ranging from 0.08°C to 0.12°C, indicating impressive temperature stability and
uniformity across the measurement locations. With the manufacturer specified accuracy
of ±0.2°C, the SHT30 sensors provide reliable temperature feedback for the PID con-
trol system. The low variability between sensors validates the effectiveness of the multi
zone heating approach and confirms that temperature gradients across the enclosure are
minimal.
The Optris infrared camera measurements have a large accuracy specification of ”±2°C
or ±2%, whichever is greater”[31]. Indicating that it has an accuracy ±2°C even though
the percentage specification resulting in ±0.6°C at 30°C would fit the acquired data. The
temperature distribution gradient across the inner backplate, shows a higher standard
deviation of 0.47°C and a mean temperature of 30.16°C.
The camera captures temperature variations across different surfaces and locations, in-
cluding areas influenced by airflow patterns, heat source proximity, and surface emissivity
variations. The broader distribution range (29.30°C to 31.40°C across all tests) provides
temperature gradients that point measurements cannot detect, confirming that tempera-
ture uniformity is maintained throughout the working volume rather than just at discrete
sensor locations.
The box plot analysis demonstrates that all three measurement has data around the target
temperature of 30°C, with the actual measured values falling well within the MVP criteria
of ±1°C temperature uniformity. The SHT30 sensors show the smallest distribution of
actual measurements, confirming their suitability as primary control feedback sensors.
The infrared camera, while showing greater variance, provides essential validation that
temperature uniformity extends across the inner backplate of the enclosure.
This validation of temperature estimation methods confirms several key performance char-
acteristics:
• Temperature stability: All methods show mean temperatures within 0.19°C of
the target setpoint.
• Design validation: Simulation results confirm the theoretical integrity of the ther-
mal enclosure design.
• Control precision: SHT30 sensors demonstrate suﬀicient precision for accurate
PID control and logging.
• Temperature uniformity: IR camera mapping confirms uniform temperature dis-
tribution across the back surface.
The analysis demonstrates that the enclosure successfully maintains temperature unifor-
mity within specified criteria, with the SHT30 sensors providing reliable control feed-
back and the IR camera confirming a uniform temperature distribution. This integrated
measurement approach provides confidence in both current performance and long-term
reliability of the incubation system.

This project has successfully met the majority of the MVP criteria set out at the begin-
ning. An enclosure was successfully designed and built using robust design principles and
suitable materials. Multiple cutouts for intersecting plates and components were made
to reduce the number of visible screws, resulting in an easy to clean and maintain en-
closure. The sandwich design with outer and inner plates makes this possible, while also
allowing for an estimated effective insulation property with a heat loss around 119W at
a 20°C temperature difference using ROCKWOOL Prolit Lamella Mat insulation. This
allows the system to operate more consistently and eﬀiciently at the desired temperature
setpoint, while also enhancing temperature uniformity by reducing any cold spots.
The temperature range is suﬀicient for the most viable sample growth applications, and
the uniformity of the temperature is within acceptable limits. Heating of the enclosure
showed to be effective, with the temperature reaching the upper limit of growing sample
requirements at 37°C, with above temperatures being available to eliminate contamination
and microorganisms. Cooling was limited due to power supply and fins to air convection
constraints. The Peltier device cools down the fins in a short period, until the power
supply turns down its current supply. The humidifiers are working, but safety concerns
arose under the electronic assembly, as the power supplies sit next to the humidifiers,
which can cause short circuits if water leaks occur. The system verifies its temperature
and humidity with six sensors, three on the top and three on the bottom, ensuring accurate
readings. This allows multiple PID controllers within the enclosure to use feedback loops
to adjust temperature and humidity based on the readings, ensuring that the whole system
operates within the desired parameters.
The GUI developed for this project is user-friendly and allows easy monitoring and ad-
justment of parameters using a touchscreen. It provides a clear overview of the current
temperature and humidity levels, as well as the ability to set desired values.
Logging
functionality is also implemented, allowing users to track changes over time and analyze
Modular Incubation and Monitoring System for Sample Storage in High-Throughput Screening
99
==PAGE:108==
performance. Light and UV light sterilization features are included, with UV light showing
significant effectiveness in reducing contamination levels.
The system modular framework is designed to allow for new features or functionalities as
needed. Simple plug and play modules can be added or removed to the system using pogo
pins with T-tracks. During this project, a few modules were developed to demonstrate
the modularity and some of its capabilities, including a storage module, a present plate
module, and a dot module. The storage module allows for the storage of samples even
with different plate geometries. The present plate module allows the system to cooperate
with other systems or even stack multiple systems side by side, for another robot to pick
up. The dot module is a simple development module that can be used to mount custom
components or sensors, allowing for easy integration of future technologies and function-
alities. This modular design ensures that the system can adapt to changing requirements
and incorporate advancements in technology without requiring a complete redesign.
Overall, the test and evaluation phase demonstrates that the enclosure meets its primary
functional requirements and is well suited for controlled sample incubation and experimen-
tation. While some limitations were identified, particularly regarding cooling performance
and humidifier placement, these are well understood and can be addressed in future it-
erations with improved power supplies and enhanced safety measures. The modular and
expandable design, combined with reliable temperature and humidity control, provides a
strong foundation for further development and integration of additional features.

9
Future work
The project has shown that the climate-controlled modular enclosure system is a capable
platform to test different modules and climate conditions for biological samples. However,
there are still some issues that need to be addressed in order to make the system more
reliable and easier to use. This chapter will discuss some of the issues that were encountered
during the project and suggest solutions for future iterations of the system.
9.1
Power supply and cooling
High power consumption during cooling proved problematic as described in chapter 8.
Three suﬀicient power supplies at different voltages were purchased, but they appeared
insuﬀicient and unreliable in delivering power. Safety was not a concern, as current mea-
surements showed that they throttled output, indicating built-in safety features, resulting
in the system being unresponsive when cooling.
The 12V power supply found in the lab was insuﬀicient and adding a 4A supply in parallel
provided only marginal improvement. Therefore, it is recommended to test a power supply
with at least 25A to ensure adequate headroom and assess whether cooling performance
improves. A cooling test from 37°C to 25°C with and without activated Peltier elements
could help determine if the current setup is effective. If not, a solution offering greater
cold surface area for air cooling should be considered. However, the added complexity,
space, and high current demands of a more eﬀicient cooling system may not be justified
unless ambient temperatures regularly exceed 25°C or temperatures below is required.
The 5V power supply is mainly used to power the Raspberry Pi, modular framework,
sensors and the GPIO PWM signals controlling the system. The supply could easily handle
the connected modules, but occasional instability was observed, with the Raspberry Pi
displaying low-voltage warnings. This issue is common with Raspberry Pi devices, as they
are sensitive to third-party power supplies, and could be resolved by selecting a more
stable and reputable supply.
The 24V power supply with 45A powered the heating system, fans, LED light and UV
light, which showed no obvious issues under testing. The supply was stable and the system
ran for a long time without heating issues, likely due to its large headroom. The current
output could not be tested as the multimeter was unable to measure more than around
10A. The only thing noticed was that the power supply expelled a lot of heat to the left,
which the left heater PID controller was able to adjust for.
For future iterations, it is recommended to select stable and reputable power supplies,
even if they come at a higher cost, and to ensure that each supply has suﬀicient power
rating headroom to prevent system throttling.
The electronic compartment in the back should for future iterations be separated from
the rest of the airflow system. This decoupling should be done to avoid unwanted high
humidity and condensation within the electronics, while keeping the heating from the
power supplies and the Raspberry Pi away from the airflow system and out of an exhaust
to the ambient air. It can be done by reorganizing the climate units or removing the middle
climate unit to allow for a walled-off compartment for the electronics in the center. The
test and earlier calculations showed that the system can be handled with fewer climate
units, at the cost of lower heating and cooling capacity with a slower time response. An
Modular Incubation and Monitoring System for Sample Storage in High-Throughput Screening
101
==PAGE:110==
opening in the middle of the electronic backplate could allow for ambient airflow to the
electronics compartment.
If a major system redesign is made and cooling is considered a requirement, allocating ad-
ditional space for a more advanced cooling solution should be considered. A commercially
available mini HVAC system could be considered for a more premium price, which would
allow for a more eﬀicient cooling system. Systems such as the DC Aircon DV3220E-AC/H
from RIGID HVAC CO., LTD. could be considered, as it provides a fully functioning VCR
cooling and PTC heater system with a capacity of 550W that can be controlled by the
Raspberry Pi. This, in conjunction with the ultrasonic humidifier, would allow for an
effective adjustable climate system. However, this would be more expensive, require a lot
of space, and lead to a compromise in the overall design, which is why this option was not
chosen in the Pugh matrix for the climate in Table 4.1.
In summary, addressing the power supply and cooling challenges is essential for improving
the reliability and performance of the climate system. By investing in higher quality power
supplies, future iterations can achieve greater stability, eﬀiciency, and adaptability to a
wider range of experimental conditions.
9.2
Humidity control
The humidifiers showed an effective change in humidity when individually tested. One in
each climate module is integrated and connected to the Raspberry Pi, but it appeared to
lack voltage from the GPIO PWM power signal. The 3.3V power signal only gave small
amounts of water vapor, which was not enough to change the humidity in the system. A
voltage step-up converter or an optocoupler equipped MOSFET should be used to increase
the PWM voltage to 5V. It was initially not implemented, as the humidifier stated that
it could run on battery power, which would be down to 3V.
To ensure a reliable distilled water supply to the humidifier, a peristaltic pump was partly
integrated and tested with the right climate unit containing the ultrasonic humidifier.
Access holes were added to the climate unit to accommodate silicone tubing, allowing the
pump to be positioned externally and deliver water as needed. The pump, powered by a 5V
supply, successfully transported water through the tubing into the ultrasonic humidifier’s
reservoir. Its operation relies on compressing the silicone tube to create a vacuum, which
draws water through the tube and prevents both backflow and direct contact with the
water. The pump can be controlled directly by the Raspberry Pi via GPIO pins using a
PWM signal. A large external water tank should be added to serve as the main reservoir,
supplying distilled water to the system as required. To prevent overflow, an additional
safety outlet is provided in the humidifier’s buffer tank, allowing excess water to return
to the main tank if necessary. This feature prevents accidental water spills in the event of
pump malfunction.
The ultrasonic humidifier demonstrated effective performance during testing and is rec-
ommended for use in future system iterations. Its operation should be both reliable and
safe, provided that the electronics compartment is properly isolated from the climate units
and the airflow system as previously described.
9.3
Wiring and simplifying
In order to simplify the system and reduce the number of solder joints, it should be
considered to order pogo pins with longer wires and already soldered USB headers from
the manufacturer. This will reduce the number of solders and components, making the
system more reliable and easier to assemble.
102
Modular Incubation and Monitoring System for Sample Storage in High-Throughput Screening
==PAGE:111==
A total of 12 wires are used for each climate unit, which is connected to five control boards.
The controlling boards are connected to a power supply and the Raspberry Pi. This results
in a lot of wires and solder joints, which can be simplified by making the climate units
into one united component with one power and control input. The climate unit is then
a separate subassembly that is easily mounted and connected to the main system. This
reduces the number of wires and solders, making the assembly of the system easier.
A lot of solder joints and wire terminals are used in the system. This is not ideal, as
it can lead to unreliable connections and make the system more diﬀicult to assemble. It
should be considered to use connectors instead of solder joints, as this will make it easier
to organize the wires and make it easier to replace components if needed.
In general, the number of wires should be reduced and better managed, by making or-
ganized cable trays and implementing cable management solutions into the design of the
system. Some cable holes and wiring channels were made, but this should be improved
and implemented for all wires in the system. This will make the system more organized
and easier to work with, as well as reducing the risk of wires getting tangled or damaged.
9.4
Ergonomic improvements
The design of the module connections prioritized ease of use, featuring satisfying ball
plunger clicks upon mounting and drafted inlets for the T-tracks. Further iterations and
refinements could enhance the smoothness of module connection and disconnection. Ad-
ditional ergonomic improvements, such as incorporating plastic padding on the handles
and reducing the overall weight of the modules, would further improve user comfort and
facilitate easier handling.
However, these enhancements are primarily qualitative and
challenging to quantify objectively.
Reducing the weight of the modules can be easily achieved by using thinner steel plates
and introducing more cutouts in the shell, as the 2mm 304 stainless steel plates used in
the current design showed good structural integrity and rigidity. The current design is
quite heavy, with each module weighing around 5kg, which can be cumbersome to handle
and assemble. Thinner plates would still provide suﬀicient strength while significantly
reducing the overall weight of the modules. While polycarbonate plates were previously
used for the door windows, they could also be considered for non-structural components
to further decrease weight. These weight savings could be applied to both the modules
and the enclosure system, which is currently quite heavy. Adding a MVP weight criterion
should be considered. Implementing such changes would simplify handling and assembly
while also reducing the overall system weight, making it easier to transport.
The machine was intended to be placed on a table similar to other biotech equipment, but
no available table matched the dimensions of the enclosure. As a result, it is currently
placed on the floor, making the touchscreen and modules diﬀicult to reach comfortably.
Future designs could address this by including optional legs with integrated cabinet storage
beneath the machine for unused plates and modules.
9.5
Robot arm implementation
The main focus of the project was to develop a modular climate system capable of testing
various modules and climate conditions. During the initial feature brainstorming to reach
MVP, as shown in Figure 1.2, the integration of the existing robot arm was proposed
for implementation after the project deadline. This integration remains planned, however
several modifications to the robot arm are necessary to make it more suitable for the
modular system.
Modular Incubation and Monitoring System for Sample Storage in High-Throughput Screening
103
==PAGE:112==
The robot arm needs a redesign as it is constructed to be asymmetric, meaning that it is
not able to reach one side of the system, resulting in the right module column not being
available. This issue should be solved by making a new iteration on the robot arm, with
the priority of making it symmetric and able to reach all modules. In order to reach this
goal, the arm should be designed to be lighter, more compact with a smaller footprint,
and have better kinematics. This will also allow the robot arm and gripper to travel much
faster, enhancing sample throughput and imaging timelapse capabilities. A sketch of the
new robot arm is shown in Figure 9.1.
The new symmetric robot arm is designed with cylindrical joints, both for the x and y
axes, with equipped belts controlled by stepper motors. The x-axis is placed inside the
bottom and top panels with a slot in the panels for the arm to move in. A brush at the
slot ensures that dust and particles are reduced from entering into the panels. For the
enclosure in this project, the rails will likely be mounted directly on top of the panels to
minimize labor time.
Figure 9.1: Sketch of the new symmetric robot arm design
The bottom x-axis, designed as a cylindrical joint, controls all movements except rotation
around the x-axis (Rx). The top x-axis locks the Rx rotation, resulting in a more precise
movement in the x-y plane compared to the previous design, where Rx was controlled
from the bottom. In Figure 9.1, the y-axis is implemented using two cylindrical joints,
although a single linear rail could also be used. Dual cylindrical joints were chosen to
minimize unwanted Rz pivoting of the arm when it is moved by the belt on the bottom
x-axis. Both the x and y axes are positioned near the doors, providing suﬀicient space for
the gripper to move along the z-axis. This arrangement allows the gripper to access both
sides of the system while accommodating the x-axis mounting points at each end.
Overall, these improvements to the robot arm are expected to significantly enhance the
system’s flexibility, precision and eﬀiciency.
By addressing limitations of the previous
asymmetric design and optimizing kinematics, the new robot arm will better support
automated sample handling tasks in future iterations of the modular climate system.
104
Modular Incubation and Monitoring System for Sample Storage in High-Throughput Screening
==PAGE:113==
10
Conclusion
This project aimed to design and develop a modular, climate controlled HTS system,
addressing key limitations in existing solutions, particularly in terms of flexibility, repro-
ducibility, and optimal sample incubation. By applying design methodologies, the project
successfully delivered a functional MVP that integrates robust enclosure design, uniform
climate control, and a modular framework for future expansion.
The enclosure was engineered with a focus on insulation, durability, and ease of mainte-
nance. By selecting 304 stainless steel and ROCKWOOL insulation, the system achieves
both cost effectiveness, reliability, and stable thermal performance. The design process
emphasized manufacturing and assembly, using DFM and DFA principles with robust
CAD modeling techniques to ensure structural integrity and ease of future modifications.
The sandwich construction with inner and outer plates, combined with systematically se-
lected insulation, minimizes heat loss and supports stable climate conditions, while also
facilitating easy cleaning with minimal crevices to prevent contamination.
Climate control was achieved through the integration of Peltier modules for cooling, PTC
heaters for heating, and ultrasonic humidifiers for humidity regulation. The distributed
climate units, combined with multiple diffusers and recirculation airflow management, re-
sulted in uniform temperature and humidity throughout the enclosure by using multiple
PID controllers. However, the implemented Peltier devices did not demonstrate significant
cooling below room temperature due to limitations in the power supply, highlighting an
area for future improvement. Furthermore, concerns about water leakage into the elec-
tronics limited the humidifier testing to a single unit, but even so, the humidity control
system proved effective, achieving a local humidity range up to 90% relative humidity
(RH) at room temperature.
CFD simulations and experimental testing confirmed that the system meets the MVP
criteria for the temperature range and uniformity, with a temperature range from room
temperature to just above 42°C and deviations estimated to be within ±1°C based on
92.5% of the four IR camera tests conducted at a 30°C setpoint.
The camera has a
poor accuracy of ±2°C, but indicates uniform gradients. The more precise temperature
sensors showed a maximum deviation of ±0.35°C at 30°C (σ = 0.12) and ±0.69°C at
37°C (σ = 0.17). These tests, which ran for three hours, indicate that the system is well
within MVP and can maintain stable and uniform climate conditions suitable for biological
experiments, supporting reproducibility and reliability in HTS workflows.
The modular framework is a central innovation of this project, enabling rapid adaptation
to diverse experimental needs. The use of pogo pin connectors and T-track fastenings al-
lows for secure, high-speed, and user-friendly module interchangeability. Several modules,
including storage, present plate, and development dot modules, were designed and tested,
demonstrating the versatility of the system and its potential for integration with external
automation platforms. This modularity not only supports current requirements, but also
ensures that the system can evolve as new technologies and experimental protocols emerge.
Electronics and software were developed to provide seamless control and monitoring of
all system components. The Raspberry Pi 5 serves as the central controller, interfacing
with sensors, actuators, and modules through a Python-based user interface built with
Kivy and KivyMD. The software architecture supports automatic module identification,
real-time climate monitoring, and intuitive user interaction, ensuring both reliability and
Modular Incubation and Monitoring System for Sample Storage in High-Throughput Screening
105
==PAGE:114==
ease of use. The systems GUI allows users to monitor and adjust parameters, log data,
and manage modules eﬀiciently, further improving usability and scalability.
Comprehensive testing and evaluation validated the performance of the system.
The
enclosure achieved effective decontamination with UV light and airflow, resulting in low
contamination levels suitable for sensitive biological experiments. The modular design
proved robust and adaptable, supporting a range of sample types and workflows. The
system’s compliance with simple manufacturing standards and its ease of assembly and
maintenance further contribute to its practical value in laboratory environments.
In summary, this project has successfully addressed the initial problem statement by
delivering an enclosed incubation system for HTS that combines controlled incubation,
modularity, and user-friendly operation with space to implement a robotic arm. The de-
sign of the system allows for future upgrades and integration of new functions, positioning
it as a flexible platform for advancing automation in life science research. The foundation
with BOM and assembly guides established by this project provides a strong basis for con-
tinued innovation of the incubation system, with opportunities for replication and further
module development to meet evolving scientific challenges. This not only demonstrates
the feasibility of a modular incubation system but also sets the foundation for future im-
provements and broader adoption in research environments. By enabling adaptability and
integration with emerging technologies, the system is well equipped to support the next
generation of automated laboratory workflows and to contribute effectively to the progress
of life science automation.

Module code for microcontroller

the following sections. The code is written in C++ and uses Arduino libraries.

Dot module code
1
// === Configurable Variables ===
2
const char* infoMessage = "Dot plate";
3
4
void setup() {
5
Serial.begin(115200);
6
delay(1000); // Give time for serial to initialize

F.3
Present plate module code
1
#include <ESP32Servo.h>
2
3
Servo myServo;
4
5
// === Configurable Variables ===
6
const char* infoMessage = "Present plate";
7
8
int inputAngle = 95;
9
10
int currentAngle = inputAngle;
// Initial position
11
int targetAngle = inputAngle;
12
13
const int servoPin = 4;
14
const int maxSpeed = 3;
// Maximum degrees per step
15
const float acceleration = 0.1; // Degrees per step²
16
float speed = 0;
17
18
void setup() {
19
myServo.attach(servoPin);
20
Serial.begin(115200);
21
delay(1000); // Give time for serial to initialize
22
23
myServo.write(currentAngle);
24
Serial.println(infoMessage);
25
}
26
27
void loop() {
28
// Check for input
29
if (Serial.available()) {
30
char input = Serial.read();
31
32
if (input == '0') {
33
targetAngle = inputAngle;
34
} else if (input == '1') {
35
targetAngle = 0;
36
} else if (input == '?') {
37
delay(100);
38
Serial.println(infoMessage);
39
}
40
}
41
42
// Smooth motion logic
43
if (currentAngle != targetAngle) {
44
int direction = (targetAngle > currentAngle) ? 1 : -1;
45
float distance = abs(targetAngle - currentAngle);
46
47
// Accelerate or decelerate
48
if (speed * speed / (2 * acceleration) < distance) {
49
speed += acceleration;
50
if (speed > maxSpeed) speed = maxSpeed;
51
} else {
52
speed -= acceleration;
53
if (speed < 0.5) speed = 0.5; // Minimum speed to prevent stalling
54
}
55
56
currentAngle += direction * round(speed);
57
if ((direction == 1 && currentAngle > targetAngle) ||
58
(direction == -1 && currentAngle < targetAngle)) {
59
currentAngle = targetAngle;
60
}
61
158
Modular Incubation and Monitoring System for Sample Storage in High-Throughput Screening
==PAGE:167==
62
myServo.write(currentAngle);
63
delay(20); // Adjust timing for smoothness
64
}
65
}
